-- Recipe 01: Hello World
-- Minimal SPL program — verify spl + adapter + model work.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/01_hello_world/hello.spl
--   spl run cookbook/01_hello_world/hello.spl --adapter ollama
--   spl run cookbook/01_hello_world/hello.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3

PROMPT hello_world
SELECT
    system_role('You are a friendly assistant. Introduce yourself and SPL 2.0 in two sentences.')
GENERATE greeting()

-- Recipe 02: Ollama Proxy
-- General-purpose LLM query — proxy any prompt to any adapter/model.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 prompt="Explain quantum computing in one sentence"
--   spl run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter ollama -m llama3.2 prompt="Write a haiku about coding"
--   spl run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter ollama -m mistral prompt="What is 2+2?"
--   spl run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter anthropic prompt="Summarise the history of AI"
--
-- Parameters:
--   prompt  (required)  The user prompt / question

PROMPT ollama_proxy
SELECT
    system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
    context.prompt AS prompt
GENERATE answer(prompt)
```

     | ============================================================
     | Model: gemma3

-- Recipe 03: Multilingual Greeting
-- Greet in any language — demonstrates parametric context with lang.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/03_multilingual/multilingual.spl --adapter ollama user_input="hello wen" lang="Chinese"
--   spl run cookbook/03_multilingual/multilingual.spl --adapter ollama user_input="hello wen" lang="French"
--   spl run cookbook/03_multilingual/multilingual.spl --adapter ollama user_input="hello wen" lang="Japanese"
--   spl run cookbook/03_multilingual/multilingual.spl --adapter ollama user_input="hello wen" lang="Spanish"

PROMPT multilingual_greeting
SELECT
    system_role('You are a friendly assistant. Respond in the language specified.'),
    context.user_input AS input,
    context.lang AS lang
GENERATE greeting(input, lang)
```

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 04: Model Showdown
-- Same prompt to multiple Ollama models — compare responses side-by-side.
-- Uses parallel CTEs instead of a bash loop for a pure-SPL implementation.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/04_model_showdown/showdown.spl --adapter ollama \
--       prompt="What is the meaning of life?"
--   spl run cookbook/04_model_showdown/showdown.spl --adapter ollama \
--       prompt="Explain recursion in 3 sentences" \
--       model_1=gemma3 model_2=llama3.2 model_3=mistral

CREATE FUNCTION compare_responses(
    prompt TEXT,
    model_1 TEXT, answer_1 TEXT,
    model_2 TEXT, answer_2 TEXT,
    model_3 TEXT, answer_3 TEXT
) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a neutral evaluator comparing responses from three AI models.

Prompt given to all models: "{prompt}"

First, reproduce each model's raw response exactly as given, formatted like this:

=== {model_1} ===
{answer_1}

=== {model_2} ===
{answer_2}

=== {model_3} ===
{answer_3}

Then provide your evaluation. For each model briefly assess:
- Response quality
- Key strengths or weaknesses

Conclude with which model gave the most helpful answer and why.
$$;

WORKFLOW model_showdown
    INPUT:
        @prompt  TEXT DEFAULT 'What is the meaning of life?',
        @model_1 TEXT DEFAULT 'gemma3',
        @model_2 TEXT DEFAULT 'phi3',
        @model_3 TEXT DEFAULT 'qwen2.5'
    OUTPUT: @comparison TEXT
DO
    -- Run the same prompt against all three models in parallel CTEs
    WITH
        response_1 AS (
            PROMPT ask_model_1
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                @prompt AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        response_2 AS (
            PROMPT ask_model_2
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                @prompt AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_2
        ),
        response_3 AS (
            PROMPT ask_model_3
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                @prompt AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_3
        )
    SELECT
        response_1.answer AS answer_1,
        response_2.answer AS answer_2,
        response_3.answer AS answer_3
    INTO @answer_1, @answer_2, @answer_3

    -- Judge all three responses together
    GENERATE compare_responses(
        @prompt,
        @model_1, @answer_1,
        @model_2, @answer_2,
        @model_3, @answer_3
    ) INTO @comparison

    COMMIT @comparison WITH
        status   = 'complete',
        model_1  = @model_1,
        model_2  = @model_2,
        model_3  = @model_3

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        COMMIT 'One or more models failed to respond.' WITH status = 'error'
END
```

[05] Self-Refine

```

```spl
-- SPL 2.0: Self-Refine Pattern
-- Iteratively improves output through critique and refinement

WORKFLOW self_refine
  INPUT: 
    @task text,
    @max_iterations integer default 5
  OUTPUT: @result text
DO
  @iteration := 0
  --  @max_iterations := 5

  -- Initial draft
  GENERATE draft(@task) INTO @current

  -- Iterative refinement loop
  WHILE @iteration < @max_iterations DO
    GENERATE critique(@current) INTO @feedback

    EVALUATE @feedback
      WHEN 'satisfactory' THEN
        COMMIT @current WITH status = 'complete', iterations = @iteration
      OTHERWISE
        GENERATE refined(@current, @feedback) INTO @current
        @iteration := @iteration + 1
    END
  END

  -- If loop exhausted, commit best effort
  COMMIT @current WITH status = 'max_iterations', iterations = @iteration

EXCEPTION
  WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
    COMMIT @current WITH status = 'partial'
  WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
    COMMIT @current WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 3
     | Tokens: 368 in / 246 out

Binary rhythms entwine
Minds in virtual dance

```spl
-- SPL 2.0: ReAct Agent — Population Growth
-- Fetch a country's population for the past two years via WebSearch,
-- then compute year-over-year growth rate using a Python tool (no LLM math).
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/06_react_agent/react_agent.spl \
--       --adapter claude_cli -m claude-sonnet-4-6 \
--       --allowed-tools WebSearch \
--       --tools cookbook/06_react_agent/tools.py \
--       country="France"

-- Tight prompt for search_population: return only the number, no narration.
CREATE FUNCTION search_population(country TEXT, year INT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Use WebSearch to find the total population of {country} for the year {year}.

Return ONLY the population as a plain integer with no commas, spaces, or text.
Example of valid output: 1409670000
No explanation. No units. No markdown. Just the number.
$$;

-- SPL PROCEDURE: search the web for a country's population in a specific year.
PROCEDURE search_population(country TEXT, year INT)
RETURNS TEXT
DO
  GENERATE search_population(country, year) INTO @result
  COMMIT @result
END

-- calc_growth_rate is a Python tool registered from tools.py via --tools.
-- CALL routes to Python directly — deterministic, zero LLM cost.

-- Tight prompt for growth_report: plain narrative, no tool use, no file writes.
CREATE FUNCTION growth_report(country TEXT, year_prev TEXT, pop_prev TEXT, year_curr TEXT, pop_curr TEXT, growth_rate TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Write a concise 2-3 sentence population growth report for {country}.

Data:
- {year_prev} population: {pop_prev}
- {year_curr} population: {pop_curr}
- Year-over-year growth rate: {growth_rate}%

Plain prose only. No markdown headers. No file creation. No tool use. Just the narrative.
$$;

WORKFLOW population_growth
  INPUT: 
    @country TEXT DEFAULT 'China',
    @year_curr integer default 2023
  OUTPUT: @report TEXT
DO
  @year_prev := @year_curr - 1
  -- @year_curr := 2024

  -- Step 1: Fetch population for each year via WebSearch
  CALL search_population(@country, @year_prev) INTO @pop_prev
  CALL search_population(@country, @year_curr) INTO @pop_curr

  -- Step 2: Compute growth rate — routed to Python tool, not LLM
  CALL calc_growth_rate(@pop_prev, @pop_curr) INTO @growth_rate

  -- Step 3: Compose the final human-readable report
  GENERATE growth_report(@country, @year_prev, @pop_prev, @year_curr, @pop_curr, @growth_rate) INTO @report

  COMMIT @report WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
  WHEN SearchFailed THEN
    GENERATE search_error_report(@country) INTO @report
    COMMIT @report WITH status = 'error'
END
```

     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 3
     | Tokens: 186 in / 113 out
     | Latency: 46181ms

France's population grew from approximately 68.1 million in 2022 to 68.3 million in 2023, an increase of roughly 222,000 people. This represents a modest year-over-year growth rate of 0.33%, consistent with the slow demographic expansion typical of developed Western European nations. While the population continues to rise, the subdued growth rate reflects broader trends of declining birth rates and aging demographics across the region.
```

```prompt
Explain how encryption works
```
     | 
```spl
-- SPL 2.0: Safe Generation with Exception Handling
-- Demonstrates LLM-specific error handling patterns

WORKFLOW safe_generation
  INPUT: @prompt text
  OUTPUT: @result text
  SECURITY: CLASSIFICATION: internal
DO
  GENERATE response(@prompt) INTO @result

  -- Quality check via semantic evaluation (LLM-as-judge)
  GENERATE quality_assess(@result) INTO @quality

  EVALUATE @quality
    WHEN 'high_quality' THEN
      COMMIT @result WITH status = 'high_quality'
    WHEN 'acceptable' THEN
      GENERATE improved(@result, @prompt) INTO @result
      COMMIT @result WITH status = 'refined'
    OTHERWISE
      COMMIT @result WITH status = 'best_effort'
  END

EXCEPTION
  WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
    GENERATE response(@prompt) INTO @result
    COMMIT @result WITH status = 'conservative'

  WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
    RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1

  WHEN RefusalToAnswer THEN
    COMMIT @prompt WITH status = 'refused'

  WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
    COMMIT @result WITH status = 'truncated'
END
```
     | 
     | ============================================================
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 3
     | Tokens: 877 in / 756 out
     | Latency: 9389ms
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
```output
Encryption is the process of converting plaintext (readable data) into ciphertext (unreadable data) to protect it from unauthorized access. Here's a step-by-step explanation of how encryption works:

1. **Key Generation**: A key is generated, which is used for encryption and decryption. The key can be a random number or a secret phrase.
2. **Encryption Algorithm**: An encryption algorithm, such as AES (Advanced Encryption Standard) or RSA, is applied to the plaintext data. The algorithm takes the plaintext data and the key as input and produces ciphertext as output.
3. **Ciphertext Generation**: The encryption algorithm scrambles the plaintext data using the key, making it unreadable without the decryption key.
4. **Output**: The resulting ciphertext is stored or transmitted over a communication channel.

There are two main types of encryption:

1. **Symmetric Encryption**: This type of encryption uses the same key for both encryption and decryption. Examples include AES and DES (Data Encryption Standard).
2. **Asymmetric Encryption**: This type of encryption uses a pair of keys: one public key for encryption and a private key for decryption. RSA (Rivest-Shamir-Adleman) is an example of asymmetric encryption.

**Decryption Process**

To decrypt the ciphertext, the recipient uses the corresponding decryption key to reverse the encryption algorithm's steps:

1. **Ciphertext Input**: The ciphertext data is fed into the decryption algorithm.
2. **Decryption Algorithm**: The decryption algorithm applies the inverse operations of the encryption algorithm to the ciphertext, using the same key used for encryption.
3. **Plaintext Output**: The resulting plaintext data is extracted and can now be read.

Encryption ensures that only authorized individuals with the correct decryption key can access the encrypted data, providing a basic level of security for data protection.
```
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
     | Variables:
     |   @prompt = Explain how encryption works

-- Recipe 08: RAG Query
-- Retrieval-augmented generation over indexed documents.
--
-- Setup:
--   pip install numpy faiss-cpu
--   spl rag add /home/gongai/projects/digital-duck/zinets/who-is-wen.md
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="Where did Wen grow up?"
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="What is Momagrid and why was it built?"
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="What is the Gang of Four in the ZiNets research team?"
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="What is eta_AI and how is it measured?"
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="How did Wen transition from physics to software engineering?"

PROMPT rag_answer
SELECT
    system_role('You are a knowledgeable assistant. Use the provided context to answer accurately.'),
    rag.query(context.question, top_k=3) AS background,
    context.question AS question
GENERATE answer(question)
```

```spl
-- Recipe 09: Chain of Thought
-- Multi-step reasoning: Research → Analyze → Summarize
-- Each GENERATE feeds into the next via workflow variables.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/09_chain_of_thought/chain.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic="distributed AI inference"
--   spl run cookbook/09_chain_of_thought/chain.spl --adapter ollama -m llama3.2 topic="quantum computing"

WORKFLOW chain_of_thought
    INPUT: @topic TEXT
    OUTPUT: @summary TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Research
    GENERATE research(@topic) INTO @research

    -- Step 2: Analyze
    GENERATE analyze(@research) INTO @analysis

    -- Step 3: Summarize
    GENERATE summarize(@analysis) INTO @summary

    COMMIT @summary WITH status = 'complete'
END
```

     | 
     | Artificial intelligence (AI) has become an integral part of modern technology, with applications in various fields such as computer vision, natural language processing, and decision-making. However, the increasing complexity and size of AI models have led to significant challenges in terms of scalability, performance, and accuracy.
     | 
     | Distributed AI inference is a technique that aims to address these challenges by distributing the computational load across multiple devices or nodes, thereby improving the overall efficiency and effectiveness of AI models. In this research overview, we will delve into the concept of distributed AI inference, its types, benefits, and applications.
     | 
     | Types of Distributed AI Inference:
     | 
     | 1. **Data Parallelism**: This approach involves splitting the input data into smaller chunks and processing each chunk on a separate device or node.
     | 2. **Model Parallelism**: This approach involves splitting the AI model into smaller sub-models and processing each sub-model on a separate device or node.
     | 3. **Parameter Server Architecture (PSA)**: This approach involves using a central server to store the model parameters, while multiple devices or nodes process the input data and send the results back to the server for aggregation.
     | 
     | Benefits of Distributed AI Inference:
     | 
     | 1. **Improved Scalability**: Distributed AI inference enables the processing of large amounts of data and complex models on multiple devices or nodes, making it possible to tackle real-world problems.
     | 2. **Enhanced Performance**: By distributing the computational load, distributed AI inference can reduce the time required for model training and testing, leading to faster results.
     | 3. **Increased Accuracy**: Distributed AI inference can improve the accuracy of AI models by reducing the impact of individual devices or nodes on the overall result.
     | 
     | Applications of Distributed AI Inference:
     | 
     | 1. **Computer Vision**: Distributed AI inference is widely used in computer vision applications such as object detection, image classification, and facial recognition.
     | 2. **Natural Language Processing (NLP)**: Distributed AI inference can be applied to NLP tasks such as text classification, sentiment analysis, and language translation.
     | 3. **Healthcare**: Distributed AI inference has the potential to revolutionize healthcare by analyzing large amounts of medical data and improving diagnosis accuracy.
     | 
     | Challenges in Distributed AI Inference:
     | 
     | 1. **Communication Overhead**: The communication between devices or nodes can lead to increased latency and bandwidth requirements.
     | 2. **Synchronization**: Synchronizing the results from multiple devices or nodes can be challenging, especially when dealing with complex models.
     | 3. **Data Distribution**: Distributing data evenly across devices or nodes can be difficult, especially when working with large datasets.
     | 
     | Future Directions:
     | 
     | 1. **Edge AI**: Distributed AI inference has the potential to enable edge AI, which involves processing data at the edge of the network rather than in the cloud.
     | 2. **Autonomous Systems**: Distributed AI inference can be used to develop autonomous systems that can make decisions and take actions without human intervention.
     | 3. **Explainability and Transparency**: Developing explainable and transparent models is crucial for ensuring trust and accountability in distributed AI inference.

[10] Batch Test
     cmd : spl run ./10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama

```bash
spl run ./10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3
```
     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 10: Batch Test
-- Automated testing of key cookbook recipes across multiple models.
-- Uses parallel CTEs to fan out all recipe × model combinations at once,
-- then generates a structured PASS/FAIL report.
--
-- The bash script (batch_test.sh) is kept for CI/CD and shell-based pipelines.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama
--   spl run cookbook/10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama \
--       model_1=gemma3 model_2=mistral

CREATE FUNCTION summarize_results(
    model_1 TEXT,
    model_2 TEXT,
    r1_name TEXT, r1_m1 TEXT, r1_m2 TEXT,
    r2_name TEXT, r2_m1 TEXT, r2_m2 TEXT,
    r3_name TEXT, r3_m1 TEXT, r3_m2 TEXT
) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a test reporter. Review the outputs below and produce a concise pass/fail report.

A result is PASS if the output is a non-empty, coherent response.
A result is FAIL if the output is empty, contains an error, or is clearly nonsensical.

Models under test: {model_1}, {model_2}

Recipe: {r1_name}
  {model_1}: {r1_m1}
  {model_2}: {r1_m2}

Recipe: {r2_name}
  {model_1}: {r2_m1}
  {model_2}: {r2_m2}

Recipe: {r3_name}
  {model_1}: {r3_m1}
  {model_2}: {r3_m2}

Format each line as:
  PASS  recipe_name  ({model})
  FAIL  recipe_name  ({model})

End with a summary line: "Results: X/Y passed, Z failed"
$$;

WORKFLOW batch_test
    INPUT:
        @model_1 TEXT DEFAULT 'gemma3',
        @model_2 TEXT DEFAULT 'llama3.2'
    OUTPUT: @report TEXT
DO
    -- Fan out all recipe × model combinations in parallel CTEs
    WITH
        -- Recipe 01: Hello World
        hello_m1 AS (
            PROMPT hello_world
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful assistant. Say hello briefly.')
            GENERATE greeting()
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        hello_m2 AS (
            PROMPT hello_world
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful assistant. Say hello briefly.')
            GENERATE greeting()
            USING MODEL @model_2
        ),

        -- Recipe 02: Ollama Proxy
        proxy_m1 AS (
            PROMPT ollama_proxy
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                'hello' AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        proxy_m2 AS (
            PROMPT ollama_proxy
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                'hello' AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_2
        ),

        -- Recipe 03: Multilingual
        multi_m1 AS (
            PROMPT multilingual
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a multilingual assistant.'),
                'hello' AS user_input,
                'English' AS lang
            GENERATE response(user_input, lang)
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        multi_m2 AS (
            PROMPT multilingual
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a multilingual assistant.'),
                'hello' AS user_input,
                'English' AS lang
            GENERATE response(user_input, lang)
            USING MODEL @model_2
        )

    SELECT
        hello_m1.greeting  AS hello_m1,  hello_m2.greeting  AS hello_m2,
        proxy_m1.answer    AS proxy_m1,  proxy_m2.answer    AS proxy_m2,
        multi_m1.response  AS multi_m1,  multi_m2.response  AS multi_m2
    INTO @hello_m1, @hello_m2,
         @proxy_m1, @proxy_m2,
         @multi_m1, @multi_m2

    -- Generate the structured pass/fail report
    GENERATE summarize_results(
        @model_1, @model_2,
        '01_hello_world/hello.spl',        @hello_m1, @hello_m2,
        '02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl',        @proxy_m1, @proxy_m2,
        '03_multilingual/multilingual.spl', @multi_m1, @multi_m2
    ) INTO @report

    COMMIT @report WITH
        status  = 'complete',
        model_1 = @model_1,
        model_2 = @model_2

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        COMMIT 'Batch test failed during generation.' WITH status = 'error'
END
```

     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 7
     | Tokens: 526 in / 279 out
     | Latency: 4057ms

  03_multilingual/multilingual.spl (gemma3)

Results: 1/4 passed, 3 failed

[11] Debate Arena

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 11: Debate Arena
-- Two LLM personas argue opposing sides of a topic, then a judge picks the winner.
-- Demonstrates multi-perspective reasoning, adversarial generation, and semantic evaluation.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/11_debate_arena/debate.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic="AI should be open-sourced"
--   spl run cookbook/11_debate_arena/debate.spl --adapter ollama -m llama3.2 topic="Remote work is better than office work"
--   spl run cookbook/11_debate_arena/debate.spl --adapter ollama topic="Tabs are better than spaces"

WORKFLOW debate_arena
    INPUT: 
        @topic TEXT,
        @max_rounds integer DEFAULT 3
    OUTPUT: @verdict TEXT
DO
    @round := 0
    -- @max_rounds := 3
    @pro_history := ''
    @con_history := ''

    -- Opening statements
    GENERATE pro_argument(@topic, 'opening statement') INTO @pro
    GENERATE con_argument(@topic, 'opening statement') INTO @con

    @pro_history := @pro
    @con_history := @con

    -- Rebuttal rounds
    WHILE @round < @max_rounds DO
        -- Pro rebuts Con's latest point
        GENERATE pro_argument(@topic, @con_history) INTO @pro_rebuttal
        @pro_history := @pro_history + '\n---\n' + @pro_rebuttal

        -- Con rebuts Pro's latest point
        GENERATE con_argument(@topic, @pro_history) INTO @con_rebuttal
        @con_history := @con_history + '\n---\n' + @con_rebuttal

        @round := @round + 1
    END

    -- Judge evaluates both sides
    GENERATE judge_debate(@topic, @pro_history, @con_history) INTO @verdict

    COMMIT @verdict WITH status = 'complete', rounds = @round

EXCEPTION
    WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
        GENERATE judge_debate(@topic, @pro_history, @con_history) INTO @verdict
        COMMIT @verdict WITH status = 'partial'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT @pro_history WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```
     |

     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 9
     | Tokens: 10191 in / 4239 out
     | Latency: 66841ms
     | ------------------------------------------------------------

Here is the revised version with the requested changes:

**Con Argument on Open-Sourcing AI**

**Introduction**

The debate around open-sourcing AI code has gained momentum in recent years. While some argue that making AI code available to the public would accelerate progress and ensure transparency, others believe it could lead to significant risks and challenges. In this con argument, we will explore the potential drawbacks of open-sourcing AI.

**Argument 1: Increased Complexity and Maintenance**

Open-sourcing AI code often leads to a proliferation of competing versions and implementations. This can result in increased complexity and maintenance burdens for developers, making it harder to keep up with updates, fix bugs, or ensure compatibility across different platforms.

**Argument 2: Security Risks and Data Leaks**

Making sensitive data and models publicly available through open-sourcing increases the risk of security breaches and data leaks. This can compromise user trust, reputation, and even national security interests. Moreover, open-sourced code may be vulnerable to cyberattacks or exploited by malicious actors.

**Argument 3: Patent Infringement and Intellectual Property Concerns**

Open-sourcing AI code without proper licensing agreements can lead to patent infringement issues, intellectual property disputes, and costly lawsuits. This can stifle innovation, dissuade developers from contributing to open-source projects, and create a toxic environment for collaboration.

**Argument 4: Lack of Control and Standardization**

Open-sourcing AI code often lacks standardization, leading to incompatibility issues with existing systems or other open-sourced models. This can result in wasted resources, delayed development, and decreased user adoption. Furthermore, the lack of control over the direction of the project can lead to inconsistent implementations and a fragmentation of the ecosystem.

**Argument 5: Monopolization and Market Disruption**

Open-sourcing AI code can lead to monopolization and market disruption, as companies may be forced to adapt to open-source models or risk losing competitiveness. This can result in job losses, business closures, and economic instability. Moreover, the lack of control over intellectual property rights can give large corporations an unfair advantage over smaller competitors.

**Counterargument**

While some argue that open-sourcing AI code promotes transparency and collaboration, it is essential to consider the potential risks and challenges associated with this approach. By acknowledging these concerns, we can design more effective models that balance openness with practicality, control, security, and intellectual property protection.

**Conclusion**

The decision to open-source AI code must be carefully considered, taking into account the potential consequences on businesses, developers, users, and society as a whole. While the benefits of open-sourcing may be significant, they do not outweigh the risks and challenges associated with this approach. By acknowledging these concerns, we can create a more nuanced understanding of the pros and cons of open-sourcing AI and make informed decisions about its future.

In addition to the suggestions I provided earlier, here are some further points that could be included in the con argument against open-sourcing AI:

1. **Address potential counter-counterarguments**: Consider addressing potential counter-counterarguments to strengthen your position.
2. **Emphasize practicality**: Highlight the importance of commercial viability, control over development, standardization, and intellectual property protection in making a more convincing case against open-sourcing AI.
3. **Use real-world examples**: Include concrete examples or case studies that demonstrate the challenges associated with open-sourcing AI.
4. **Focus on the human impact**: Emphasize the potential negative consequences of open-sourcing AI on individuals, businesses, and society as a whole.

By incorporating these additional points, you can strengthen your con argument against open-sourcing AI and create a more compelling case for considering alternative approaches to accelerating research and development in the field.

     | **Argument 1: Prioritizing Commercial Viability**
     | 
     | Open-sourcing AI code may prioritize theoretical interests over commercial viability. Companies developing proprietary AI solutions need to balance innovation with profitability. If open-sourced models cannot compete in terms of performance or user experience, they may not be adopted by businesses and individuals. This could stifle the development of practical AI applications that have a tangible impact on society.
     | 
     | **Argument 2: Limited Control over Development**
     | 
     | When AI code is open-sourced, developers from various organizations and backgrounds contribute to its evolution. While this can lead to increased collaboration and innovation, it also means that control over the direction of the project may be lost. This could result in inconsistent or incompatible implementations of the same model, hindering widespread adoption.
     | 
     | **Argument 3: Inadequate Standardization**
     | 
     | The lack of standardization in open-sourced AI projects can create difficulties for users who need to integrate these models into their own systems. Without a unified framework or set of guidelines, developers may struggle to ensure compatibility and reliability across different platforms and environments.
     | 
     | **Argument 4: Risk of Incompatibility with Enterprise Systems**
     | 
     | Many organizations rely on bespoke enterprise software that integrates with other proprietary systems. Open-sourcing AI code could lead to incompatibility issues when trying to integrate these models into existing infrastructure. This would require significant investment in retraining developers, updating existing code, and adapting to new standards.
     | 
     | **Counterargument**
     | 
     | While the benefits of open-sourcing AI are undeniable, they do not necessarily outweigh the risks associated with this approach. By acknowledging the limitations of open-sourcing, we can design more effective models that balance innovation with commercial viability, control over development, standardization, and compatibility with existing systems.
     | 
     | **Conclusion**
     | 
     | The decision to open-source AI code should be carefully considered, taking into account the potential consequences on businesses, developers, and users. A balanced approach that prioritizes both openness and practicality is necessary to ensure that AI developments serve the needs of society as a whole.
     | ---
     | The provided text appears to be a well-written con argument for and against open-sourcing AI development. The strengths of this rebuttal include:
     | 
     | 1. Clear thesis statement: The rebuttal presents a clear and concise thesis statement, which sets the tone for the rest of the text.
     | 2. Organized structure: The rebuttal is organized into four main arguments, each addressing a specific concern with open-sourcing AI. This structure makes it easy to follow and understand the author's position.
     | 3. Use of counterarguments: The rebuttal includes effective counterarguments to the pro-open-sourcing stance, which helps to build a stronger case against open-sourcing AI.
     | 4. Emphasis on practicality: The rebuttal emphasizes the importance of commercial viability, control over development, standardization, and compatibility with existing systems. This focus on practicality makes the argument more relatable and applicable to real-world scenarios.
     | 
     | To improve this rebuttal, consider addressing potential areas for improvement:
     | 
     | 1. Transitions between arguments: Smoother transitions between arguments would enhance the overall flow of the text.
     | 2. Use of specific examples: Including concrete examples or case studies would make the rebuttal more convincing and engaging.
     | 3. Addressing potential counter-counterarguments: Consider addressing potential counter-counterarguments to strengthen the rebuttal.
     | 
     | Overall, this con argument presents a solid case against open-sourcing AI development. With refinement and additional support, it could become an even stronger and more persuasive rebuttal.

     | 
     | **Pro Argument on AI Open-Sourcing**
     | 
     | **Introduction**
     | 
     | The benefits of open-sourcing AI code have been gaining momentum in recent years. While some argue that making AI code available to the public would hinder its development, others believe it could accelerate progress and ensure transparency. In this pro argument, we will explore the potential advantages of open-sourcing AI.
     | 
     | **Argument 1: Accelerated Research and Development**
     | 
     | Open-sourcing AI code allows for rapid collaboration and knowledge sharing among developers worldwide. This leads to a more diverse pool of contributors, which can result in faster innovation and breakthroughs. By making AI code publicly available, researchers can build upon existing work and quickly adapt it to new problems, accelerating the pace of progress.
     | 
     | **Argument 2: Improved Transparency and Accountability**
     | 
     | Open-sourcing AI code provides transparency into the development process, allowing for better oversight and accountability. By examining open-sourced models, researchers and developers can identify areas for improvement, detect potential biases or errors, and make data-driven decisions. This increased transparency fosters trust and credibility in AI systems.
     | 
     | **Argument 3: Enhanced Collaboration and Community Engagement**
     | 
     | Open-sourcing AI code encourages collaboration among developers, researchers, and organizations worldwide. By sharing knowledge, resources, and expertise, communities can drive innovation and create a more inclusive and diverse AI ecosystem. This collaborative approach can lead to better outcomes, improved performance, and more effective solutions.
     | 
     | **Argument 4: Promoting Data Sharing and Reusability**
     | 
     | Open-sourcing AI code promotes data sharing and reusability, which is essential for advancing AI research. By making data and models publicly available, researchers can build upon existing work, reduce duplication of effort, and accelerate discovery. This increased data sharing can lead to more accurate and reliable results.
     | 
     | **Counterargument**
     | 
     | While the benefits of open-sourcing AI code are significant, some argue that they may be outweighed by concerns about intellectual property rights, data security risks, and potential patent infringement issues. However, these concerns do not necessarily preclude the benefits of open-sourcing. In fact, many open-source projects have successfully addressed these challenges through careful planning, licensing agreements, and community engagement.
     | 
     | **Conclusion**
     | 
     | The decision to open-source AI code is a deliberate choice that can lead to significant benefits for researchers, developers, and users worldwide. By embracing openness and collaboration, we can create a more vibrant and inclusive AI ecosystem that drives progress, innovation, and societal impact.

     |   @con_history = **Con Argument on AI Open-Sourcing**
     | 
     | **Introduction**
     | 
     | The debate around AI open-sourcing has been gaining momentum in recent years. While some argue that making AI code available to the public would hinder its development, others believe it could accelerate progress and ensure transparency. In this con argument, we will explore the potential drawbacks of open-sourcing AI.
     | 
     | **Argument 1: Intellectual Property Concerns**
     | 
     | By open-sourcing AI code, developers may be giving away valuable intellectual property rights. If an open-sourced model performs exceptionally well on a particular task, it could potentially outperform proprietary alternatives, leading to lost revenue for companies that have invested heavily in developing their own models.
     | 
     | **Argument 2: Data Security Risks**
     | 
     | Open-sourcing AI code can expose sensitive data and algorithms to potential security threats. If the code is not properly secured, malicious actors could exploit vulnerabilities to gain unauthorized access to confidential information or disrupt critical systems.
     | 
     | **Argument 3: Dependence on Public Funding**
     | 
     | Many open-sourced AI projects rely heavily on public funding and support. This dependence can create a vulnerability in case of funding cuts or shifts in public interest. If an open-sourced project loses its primary source of funding, it may struggle to maintain its development and support infrastructure.
     | 
     | **Argument 4: Patent Infringement**
     | 
     | Open-sourcing AI code could lead to patent infringement issues. If multiple projects build upon each other's work without proper attribution or licensing agreements, companies may find themselves at risk of infringing on patents held by others.
     | 
     | **Counterargument**
     | 
     | While these concerns are valid, they do not necessarily outweigh the benefits of open-sourcing AI. For instance, open-sourcing AI code can:
     | 
     | * Accelerate research and development
     | * Foster collaboration among developers
     | * Ensure transparency and accountability
     | 
     | By acknowledging potential drawbacks, we can work towards mitigating them and creating a more secure and equitable environment for AI development.
     | 
     | **Conclusion**
     | 
     | The decision to open-source AI code is not a simple one. While there are valid concerns, the benefits of open-sourcing far outweigh the risks in many cases. By embracing openness and collaboration, we can create a more vibrant and inclusive AI ecosystem that drives progress and innovation.

     | Here's the con_argument against the pro-open-sourcing stance:
     | 
     | **The Dark Side of Open-Sourcing AI**
     | 
     | While open-sourcing AI code may seem like a utopian solution to accelerate research and development, it poses significant risks and challenges. In this con argument, we will explore the potential downsides of open-sourcing AI.
     | 
     | **Argument 1: Increased Complexity and Maintenance**
     | 
     | Open-sourcing AI code often leads to a proliferation of competing versions and implementations. This can result in increased complexity and maintenance burdens for developers, making it harder to keep up with updates, fix bugs, or ensure compatibility across different platforms.
     | 
     | **Argument 2: Security Risks and Data Leaks**
     | 
     | Making sensitive data and models publicly available through open-sourcing increases the risk of security breaches and data leaks. This can compromise user trust, reputation, and even national security interests. Moreover, open-sourced code may be vulnerable to cyberattacks or exploited by malicious actors.
     | 
     | **Argument 3: Patent Infringement and Intellectual Property Concerns**
     | 
     | Open-sourcing AI code without proper licensing agreements can lead to patent infringement issues, intellectual property disputes, and costly lawsuits. This can stifle innovation, dissuade developers from contributing to open-source projects, and create a toxic environment for collaboration.
     | 
     | **Argument 4: Lack of Control and Standardization**
     | 
     | Open-sourcing AI code often lacks standardization, leading to incompatibility issues with existing systems or other open-sourced models. This can result in wasted resources, delayed development, and decreased user adoption. Furthermore, the lack of control over the direction of the project can lead to inconsistent implementations and a fragmentation of the ecosystem.
     | 
     | **Argument 5: Monopolization and Market Disruption**
     | 
     | Open-sourcing AI code can lead to monopolization and market disruption, as companies may be forced to adapt to open-source models or risk losing competitiveness. This can result in job losses, business closures, and economic instability. Moreover, the lack of control over intellectual property rights can give large corporations an unfair advantage over smaller competitors.
     | 
     | **Counterargument**
     | 
     | While some argue that open-sourcing AI code promotes transparency and collaboration, it is essential to consider the potential risks and challenges associated with this approach. By acknowledging these concerns, we can design more effective models that balance openness with practicality, control, security, and intellectual property protection.
     | 
     | **Conclusion**
     | 
     | The decision to open-source AI code must be carefully considered, taking into account the potential consequences on businesses, developers, users, and society as a whole. While the benefits of open-sourcing may be significant, they do not outweigh the risks and challenges associated with this approach. By acknowledging these concerns, we can create a more nuanced understanding of the pros and cons of open-sourcing AI and make informed decisions about its future.
     | 
     | In conclusion to the con argument against open-sourcing AI, consider the following additional points:
     | 
     | 1. Address potential counter-counterarguments: Consider addressing potential counter-counterarguments to strengthen your position.
     | 2. Emphasize practicality: Highlight the importance of commercial viability, control over development, standardization, and intellectual property protection in making a more convincing case against open-sourcing AI.
     | 3. Use real-world examples: Include concrete examples or case studies that demonstrate the challenges associated with open-sourcing AI.
     | 4. Focus on the human impact: Emphasize the potential negative consequences of open-sourcing AI on individuals, businesses, and society as a whole.
     | 
     | By incorporating these additional points, you can strengthen your con argument against open-sourcing AI and create a more compelling case for considering alternative approaches to accelerating research and development in the field.

     | **Introduction:**
     | 
     | In today's rapidly evolving technological landscape, Artificial Intelligence (AI) has emerged as a crucial component in shaping the future of industries and society at large. As AI continues to advance at an unprecedented pace, it is essential that its development is guided by principles that prioritize transparency, collaboration, and the public good. One such principle is open-sourcing AI.
     | 
     | **Argument 1: Open-Sourcing AI Promotes Transparency and Accountability**
     | 
     | By making AI development open-source, developers can share their code, data, and research methods with a global community of experts. This openness fosters a culture of transparency, allowing others to review, reproduce, and build upon existing work. In turn, this not only accelerates innovation but also ensures that AI systems are developed with accountability in mind.
     | 
     | **Argument 2: Open-Sourcing AI Encourages Collaboration and Knowledge Sharing**
     | 
     | Open-sourcing AI enables developers from diverse backgrounds and organizations to contribute to the development of intelligent systems. This collaborative approach facilitates knowledge sharing, skill exchange, and collective problem-solving. By working together, researchers can tackle complex challenges that might be insurmountable for any one individual or organization.
     | 
     | **Argument 3: Open-Sourcing AI Supports Public Trust and Safety**
     | 
     | As AI increasingly impacts various aspects of our lives, it is essential to ensure that these systems are developed with public safety and trust in mind. Open-sourcing AI allows regulators and civil society organizations to scrutinize the development process, identify potential risks, and advocate for responsible AI practices.
     | 
     | **Argument 4: Open-Sourcing AI Accelerates Scientific Progress**
     | 
     | Open-source AI development enables researchers to access and build upon existing research, accelerating scientific progress in fields such as computer vision, natural language processing, and machine learning. By sharing knowledge and expertise, the developer community can bypass traditional barriers to entry, fostering innovation and discovery.
     | 
     | **Conclusion:**
     | 
     | In conclusion, open-sourcing AI is an essential step towards ensuring that artificial intelligence development prioritizes transparency, collaboration, accountability, public trust, and scientific progress. As we continue to explore the vast potential of AI, it is crucial that we adopt open-source principles to create a more inclusive, responsible, and beneficial AI ecosystem for all.
     | 
     | **Recommendation:**
     | 
     | We urge developers, policymakers, and industry leaders to prioritize open-sourcing AI development, recognizing its benefits for both technical advancement and social responsibility. By working together, we can harness the power of AI to drive positive change and create a brighter future for humanity.

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 12: Plan and Execute
-- A planner agent decomposes a task into steps, then an executor implements each one.
-- When output_dir is set, generated code files are written to disk one-by-one.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/plan_execute.spl --adapter ollama \
--       --tools cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/tools.py \
--       task="Build a REST API for a todo app" \
--       output_dir="cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/output"
--
--   spl run cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/plan_execute.spl --adapter ollama \
--       task="Set up a CI/CD pipeline for a Python project"


-- Phase 1: Decompose task into a numbered implementation plan
CREATE FUNCTION plan(task TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a senior software architect. Break down this task into clear implementation steps:

Task: {task}

Output a numbered list of concrete steps (3–6 steps). Each step should be specific
and independently executable. No prose — just the numbered list.
$$;

-- Count how many steps are in the plan (capped at max_steps)
CREATE FUNCTION count_steps(plan TEXT, max_steps TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Count the number of numbered steps in this plan.
Return ONLY a single integer — no explanation, no text.
If there are more than {max_steps} steps, return {max_steps}.

Plan:
{plan}
$$;

-- Extract step N from the plan (0-indexed)
CREATE FUNCTION extract_step(plan TEXT, step_index TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Extract step number {step_index} (0-indexed) from this numbered plan and return
ONLY that step's description. No numbering prefix. No extra text.

Plan:
{plan}
$$;

-- Execute a step: produce a concise design decision + list of files to create
CREATE FUNCTION execute_step(current_step TEXT, prior_results TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a senior software engineer planning an implementation.

Step: {current_step}
Completed so far: {prior_results}

Describe in 2-3 sentences what this step produces and list the filenames it creates.
Be brief — no code yet, just the design decision and file list.
$$;

-- Validate that a step produced meaningful output
CREATE FUNCTION validate_step(current_step TEXT, step_result TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Did the following implementation successfully address the step?

Step: {current_step}
Implementation: {step_result}

Reply with exactly one word: "passed" or "failed".
$$;

-- Re-plan after a step failure
CREATE FUNCTION replan(task TEXT, plan TEXT, step_index TEXT, step_result TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
A step in our implementation plan failed. Create a revised plan.

Original task: {task}
Original plan: {plan}
Failed at step: {step_index}
Failure details: {step_result}

Output a new numbered plan starting from step {step_index}. Keep completed steps as-is.
$$;

-- Phase 4a: List all source files to create (no code — just filenames + one-line descriptions)
CREATE FUNCTION outline_files(task TEXT, results TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a senior software engineer. Based on the implementation plan below, list every
source file that must be created to complete the task.

Task: {task}

Implementation plan:
{results}

Output a numbered list — one file per line — with the filename and a one-sentence description.
Always include a README.md as the last item.
Example:
1. app.py - Flask application entry point, registers blueprints and starts the server
2. models.py - SQLAlchemy ORM models for Todo and User
3. README.md - Setup instructions, project layout, and example API calls

No code. No prose. Just the numbered file list.
$$;

-- Count files in the outline
CREATE FUNCTION count_files(outline TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Count the number of numbered items in this file list.
Return ONLY a single integer — no explanation, no text.

List:
{outline}
$$;

-- Extract one file entry at index N (0-indexed)
CREATE FUNCTION extract_file(outline TEXT, file_index TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Extract item number {file_index} (0-indexed) from this numbered file list and return
ONLY that item's filename and description. No number prefix. No extra text.

List:
{outline}
$$;

-- Generate complete code for ONE file
CREATE FUNCTION generate_file(task TEXT, results TEXT, file_desc TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a senior software engineer. Write complete, runnable code for ONE source file.

Task: {task}

Implementation plan:
{results}

File to create: {file_desc}

Output a SINGLE fenced code block. The FIRST line inside the fence MUST be a filename comment:

```python
# filename: app/main.py
...complete code...

     | 
     | Requirements:
     | - Complete, runnable code — no placeholders, no TODOs.
     | - Error handling, type hints, brief inline comments.
     | - Only this one file — nothing else.
     | $$;
     | 
     | -- Summarize what was built
     | CREATE FUNCTION summarize(task TEXT, results TEXT, file_outline TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
     | In 3-5 sentences, describe what was built for this task and how to run it.
     | 
     | Task: {task}
     | Files created:
     | {file_outline}
     | $$;
     | 
     | 
     | WORKFLOW plan_and_execute
     |     INPUT:
     |         @task TEXT DEFAULT 'Build a REST API for a todo app',
     |         @output_dir TEXT DEFAULT '',
     |         @max_steps INTEGER DEFAULT 5
     |     OUTPUT: @final_report TEXT
     | DO
     |     @step_index := 0
     |     @results := ''
     | 
     |     -- Phase 1: Plan
     |     GENERATE plan(@task) INTO @plan
     | 
     |     -- Phase 2: Count steps (capped at max_steps)
     |     GENERATE count_steps(@plan, @max_steps) INTO @step_count
     | 
     |     -- Phase 3: Execute each step (brief design notes only — no code)
     |     WHILE @step_index < @step_count DO
     |         GENERATE extract_step(@plan, @step_index) INTO @current_step
     |         GENERATE execute_step(@current_step, @results) INTO @step_result
     |         GENERATE validate_step(@current_step, @step_result) INTO @validation
     | 
     |         EVALUATE @validation
     |             WHEN 'failed' THEN
     |                 GENERATE replan(@task, @plan, @step_index, @step_result) INTO @plan
     |                 GENERATE count_steps(@plan, @max_steps) INTO @step_count
     |                 @step_index := 0
     |                 @results := ''
     |             OTHERWISE
     |                 @results := @results + '\n## Step ' + @step_index + '\n' + @step_result
     |                 @step_index := @step_index + 1
     |         END
     |     END
     | 
     |     -- Phase 4: Outline all files to generate
     |     GENERATE outline_files(@task, @results) INTO @file_outline
     |     GENERATE count_files(@file_outline) INTO @file_count
     | 
     |     -- Phase 5: Generate each file individually (one LLM call per file — avoids truncation)
     |     @file_index := 0
     |     WHILE @file_index < @file_count DO
     |         GENERATE extract_file(@file_outline, @file_index) INTO @current_file
     |         GENERATE generate_file(@task, @results, @current_file) INTO @file_code
     |         CALL write_code_files(@file_code, @output_dir) INTO @file_written
     |         @file_index := @file_index + 1
     |     END
     | 
     |     -- Phase 6: Summary report
     |     GENERATE summarize(@task, @results, @file_outline) INTO @final_report
     | 
     |     COMMIT @final_report WITH status = 'complete', steps_executed = @step_index, files_generated = @file_count
     | 
     | EXCEPTION
     |     WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
     |         GENERATE summarize(@task, @results, @file_outline) INTO @final_report
     |         COMMIT @final_report WITH status = 'partial', steps_executed = @step_index
     |     WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
     |         COMMIT @results WITH status = 'budget_limit'
     | END
     | ```

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 13: Map-Reduce Summarizer
-- Splits a large document into chunks, summarizes each chunk (map),
-- then combines all summaries into a final summary (reduce).
-- Demonstrates the map-reduce pattern for handling content beyond context limits.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/13_map_reduce/map_reduce.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 document="$(cat large_doc.txt)"
--   spl run cookbook/13_map_reduce/map_reduce.spl --adapter ollama document="$(cat README.md)" style="bullet points"
--   spl run cookbook/13_map_reduce/map_reduce.spl --adapter ollama document="long text here..." style="executive brief"

WORKFLOW map_reduce_summarizer
    INPUT: @document TEXT, @style TEXT
    OUTPUT: @final_summary TEXT
DO
    @chunk_index := 0
    @summaries := ''

    -- Step 1: Determine how to split the document
    GENERATE chunk_plan(@document) INTO @chunk_count

    -- Step 2: MAP — summarize each chunk independently
    WHILE @chunk_index < @chunk_count DO
        GENERATE extract_chunk(@document, @chunk_index, @chunk_count) INTO @chunk

        GENERATE summarize_chunk(@chunk, @chunk_index) INTO @chunk_summary

        @summaries := @summaries + '\n[Chunk ' + @chunk_index + ']: ' + @chunk_summary
        @chunk_index := @chunk_index + 1
    END

    -- Step 3: REDUCE — combine all chunk summaries into one
    GENERATE reduce_summaries(@summaries, @style) INTO @final_summary

    -- Step 4: Quality check the final summary
    GENERATE quality_score(@final_summary, @document) INTO @score

    EVALUATE @score
        WHEN > 0.7 THEN
            COMMIT @final_summary WITH status = 'complete', chunks = @chunk_count
        OTHERWISE
            GENERATE improve_summary(@final_summary, @summaries) INTO @final_summary
            COMMIT @final_summary WITH status = 'refined', chunks = @chunk_count
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
        -- If document is too large even for chunking, do hierarchical reduction
        GENERATE reduce_summaries(@summaries, @style) INTO @final_summary
        COMMIT @final_summary WITH status = 'partial'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        GENERATE reduce_summaries(@summaries, @style) INTO @final_summary
        COMMIT @final_summary WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

     | Here's a Python code snippet that demonstrates this:

```python
import re

def chunk_plan(input_text):
    # Regular expression pattern to match sentences
    sentence_pattern = r'[.!?]\s*'
    
    # Find all sentence boundaries in the text
    sentence_boundaries = re.findall(sentence_pattern, input_text)
    
    # Split the input text into chunks based on sentence boundaries
    chunks = [input_text[i:j+1] for i,j in enumerate(sentence_boundaries)]
    
    return chunks

# Test the function with the given input text
input_text = "The quick brown fox jumps over the lazy dog. This is a test document for map-reduce summarization."
chunks = chunk_plan(input_text)

for chunk in chunks:
    print(chunk)
```

```spl
-- Recipe 14: Multi-Agent Collaboration
-- Specialized agents (Researcher, Analyst, Writer) collaborate on a report.
-- Each agent has a distinct role and passes results to the next.
-- Demonstrates procedural decomposition, CALL-based delegation, and role specialization.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/14_multi_agent/multi_agent.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic="Impact of AI on healthcare"
--   spl run cookbook/14_multi_agent/multi_agent.spl --adapter ollama topic="Future of renewable energy"
--   spl run cookbook/14_multi_agent/multi_agent.spl --adapter ollama topic="State of quantum computing in 2025"

-- Agent 1: Researcher — gathers key facts and sources
PROCEDURE researcher(topic TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE research_facts(topic) INTO @facts
    GENERATE identify_key_themes(@facts) INTO @themes
    @result := @facts + '\n\nKey Themes:\n' + @themes
    COMMIT @result
END

-- Agent 2: Analyst — identifies trends, risks, and opportunities
PROCEDURE analyst(research TEXT, topic TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE analyze_trends(research) INTO @trends
    GENERATE assess_risks(research, topic) INTO @risks
    GENERATE find_opportunities(research, topic) INTO @opportunities
    @result := 'Trends:\n' + @trends + '\n\nRisks:\n' + @risks + '\n\nOpportunities:\n' + @opportunities
    COMMIT @result
END

-- Agent 3: Writer — produces polished final report
PROCEDURE writer(research TEXT, analysis TEXT, topic TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE draft_report(topic, research, analysis) INTO @draft
    GENERATE critique(@draft) INTO @feedback
    GENERATE revise_report(@draft, @feedback) INTO @final
    COMMIT @final
END

-- Orchestrator workflow
WORKFLOW multi_agent_report
    INPUT: @topic TEXT
    OUTPUT: @report TEXT
DO
    -- Delegate to specialized agents
    CALL researcher(@topic) INTO @research
    CALL analyst(@research, @topic) INTO @analysis
    CALL writer(@research, @analysis, @topic) INTO @report

    COMMIT @report WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT @research WITH status = 'partial_research_only'
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1
END
```

```output
Here is a revised version of the report incorporating the suggested changes:

**Introduction**

The impact of Artificial Intelligence (AI) on healthcare is transforming the way doctors diagnose, treat, and care for patients. As AI continues to advance, it's essential to explore its benefits, challenges, and future directions in this rapidly evolving field.

This report provides an overview of the current state of AI in healthcare, highlighting its potential to improve patient outcomes, streamline clinical workflows, and transform the way healthcare is delivered. By examining the opportunities and risks associated with AI in healthcare, we can gain a deeper understanding of how to harness its power to create better health systems.

**Benefits of AI in Healthcare**

### Enhanced Patient Outcomes

* AI-powered algorithms can analyze vast amounts of medical data to identify patterns and predict patient outcomes.
* Personalized medicine using AI-driven insights can lead to more effective treatment plans and improved patient success rates.

### Streamlined Clinical Workflows

* AI can automate routine administrative tasks, freeing up healthcare professionals to focus on high-value tasks that require human expertise.
* AI-powered chatbots and virtual assistants can help patients navigate the healthcare system and provide timely support.

### Improved Patient Engagement

* AI-driven analytics can help healthcare providers better understand patient needs and preferences.
* Personalized health coaching using AI can empower patients to take a more active role in their health.

**Challenges and Risks**

### Technical Challenges

* Ensuring data quality and accuracy is crucial for developing accurate AI models.
* Cybersecurity threats pose significant risks to the deployment of AI systems in healthcare.

### Regulatory Frameworks

* Governments must establish clear regulations and guidelines for the development and deployment of AI-powered medical devices.
* Ensuring transparency and accountability in AI decision-making is essential for building trust in these systems.

**Opportunities**

### Personalized Medicine

* AI can help tailor treatment plans to individual patients based on their unique genetic profiles, medical histories, and lifestyle factors.
* Data-driven insights from AI can guide clinicians in making more informed decisions about patient care.

### Predictive Analytics

* AI-powered predictive analytics can identify high-risk patients before they require hospitalization or other serious interventions.
* Early intervention using AI-driven insights can improve patient outcomes and reduce healthcare costs.

**Conclusion**

The impact of AI on healthcare is significant, with far-reaching implications for patient outcomes, clinical workflows, and the future of healthcare delivery. By acknowledging both the benefits and challenges associated with AI in healthcare, we can harness its power to create better health systems that prioritize patient needs.

Recommendations:

1. Encourage continued investment in AI research and development to improve the accuracy and reliability of AI-powered medical devices.
2. Establish clear regulatory frameworks for the deployment of AI in healthcare, ensuring transparency, accountability, and data quality.
3. Foster collaboration between clinicians, AI developers, policymakers, and patients to ensure that AI systems are designed with patient needs at their core.

By working together, we can unlock the full potential of AI in healthcare and create a brighter future for patients, caregivers, and healthcare providers alike.

     | 5. **Regulatory Challenges**: The rapid development and deployment of AI systems in healthcare may outpace regulatory frameworks, creating uncertainty and potential risks for patients and clinicians.
     | 6. **Dependence on Data Quality**: AI algorithms are only as good as the data they are trained on, which can lead to biased or inaccurate results if data is incomplete, inaccurate, or outdated.
     | 7. **Unintended Consequences**: The use of AI in healthcare may have unintended consequences, such as over-reliance on technology or decreased human interaction between clinicians and patients.

     | **Key Risks:**
     | 
     | 1. **Patient Safety**: The most significant risk associated with AI in healthcare is patient safety, particularly if AI-driven diagnoses or treatment recommendations lead to adverse outcomes.
     | 2. **Digital Divide**: The unequal distribution of technological infrastructure and access to AI-powered healthcare services may exacerbate existing health disparities and inequalities.
     | 
     | **Mitigation Strategies:**

```spl
-- Recipe 15: Automated Code Review
-- Multi-pass code review: security audit, performance check, style review, then synthesis.
-- Demonstrates tool-augmented analysis, multi-criteria evaluation, and structured output.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 code="$(cat myfile.py)"
--   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama code="$(cat main.go)" language="Go"
--   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama code="def foo(x): return eval(x)" language="Python"

WORKFLOW code_review
    INPUT: @code TEXT, @language TEXT
    OUTPUT: @review TEXT
DO
    -- Pass 1: Security audit
    GENERATE security_audit(@code, @language) INTO @security_findings

    -- Pass 2: Performance analysis
    GENERATE performance_review(@code, @language) INTO @perf_findings

    -- Pass 3: Code style and best practices
    GENERATE style_review(@code, @language) INTO @style_findings

    -- Pass 4: Bug detection
    GENERATE bug_detection(@code, @language) INTO @bug_findings

    -- Severity scoring for each category
    GENERATE severity_score(@security_findings) INTO @sec_score
    GENERATE severity_score(@perf_findings) INTO @perf_score
    GENERATE severity_score(@bug_findings) INTO @bug_score

    -- Synthesize all findings into a structured review
    GENERATE synthesize_review(
        @security_findings, @sec_score,
        @perf_findings, @perf_score,
        @style_findings,
        @bug_findings, @bug_score
    ) INTO @review

    -- Determine overall verdict
    EVALUATE @sec_score
        WHEN > 8 THEN
            COMMIT @review WITH status = 'critical_issues', verdict = 'block'
        WHEN > 5 THEN
            COMMIT @review WITH status = 'needs_fixes', verdict = 'request_changes'
        OTHERWISE
            COMMIT @review WITH status = 'approved', verdict = 'approve'
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
        -- Code too large — review in chunks
        GENERATE summarize_code(@code) INTO @summary
        GENERATE quick_review(@summary, @language) INTO @review
        COMMIT @review WITH status = 'partial_large_file'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT @security_findings WITH status = 'security_only'
END
```

Here's a Python solution for the task:

```python
import ast

def foo(severity_score):
    """
    Evaluates input string and returns severity score.
    
    Parameters:
    severity_score (str): A string representing severity score.
    
    Returns:
    int: Severity score value.
    """

    # Validate input string using ast.literal_eval()
    try:
        x = ast.literal_eval(repr(severity_score))
        if isinstance(x, str):
            raise ValueError("Invalid input")
        
        # Determine the severity score based on the value of x
        if x == 0:
            return "Low"
        elif x < 5:
            return "Moderate"
        else:
            return "High"
    except (ValueError, TypeError) as e:
        # Handle invalid input
        print("Invalid input")
        return None

# Example usage
severity_score = "42"
result = foo(severity_score)

if result is not None:
    print(f"Severity Score: {result}")
```

```python
import ast
import json
import yaml

def safe_eval(x):
    """
    Safely evaluates a given input using ast.literal_eval, json.loads or yaml.safe_load.
    
    Args:
        x (str): The input to be evaluated.

    Returns:
        The evaluated value if successful, otherwise raises a ValueError with an error message.
    """
    try:
        # Check if the input is a JSON string
        if x.startswith({'{', '[', '"'}):
            try:
                return json.loads(x)
            except json.JSONDecodeError as e:
                raise ValueError(f"Invalid JSON input: {x}") from e

        # Check if the input is a YAML string
        elif x.startswith({'-', '{', '['}):
            try:
                return yaml.safe_load(x)
            except yaml.YAMLError as e:
                raise ValueError(f"Invalid YAML input: {x}") from e

        # Use ast.literal_eval for other literals
        else:
            try:
                return ast.literal_eval(repr(x))
            except (ValueError, TypeError) as e:
                raise ValueError(f"Invalid literal input: {x}") from e

    except Exception as e:

def foo(severity_score):

```python
import ast
import json
import yaml

def safe_eval(x):
    """
    Safely evaluates a given input using ast.literal_eval, json.loads or yaml.safe_load.
    
    Args:
        x (str): The input to be evaluated.

    Returns:
        The evaluated value if successful, otherwise raises a ValueError with an error message.
    """
    try:
        # Check if the input is a JSON string
        if x.startswith({'{', '[', '"'}):
            try:
                return json.loads(x)
            except json.JSONDecodeError as e:
                raise ValueError(f"Invalid JSON input: {x}") from e

        # Check if the input is a YAML string
        elif x.startswith({'-', '{', '['}):
            try:
                return yaml.safe_load(x)
            except yaml.YAMLError as e:
                raise ValueError(f"Invalid YAML input: {x}") from e

        # Use ast.literal_eval for other literals
        else:
            try:
                return ast.literal_eval(x)
            except (ValueError, TypeError) as e:
                raise ValueError(f"Invalid literal input: {x}") from e

    except Exception as e:
        raise ValueError(f"Failed to evaluate input: {x}") from e


def foo(x):
    """
    Evaluates the given input using safe_eval.

    Args:
        x (str): The input to be evaluated.

    Returns:
        The evaluated value.
    """
    try:
        return safe_eval(x)
    except ValueError as e:
        print(f"Error: {e}")
        return None
```

# Test with YAML string
print(foo('--- name: John\nage: 30'))  # Output: {'name': 'John', 'age': 30}

# Test with literal string
print(foo('"Hello, World!"'))  # Output: "Hello, World!"

# Test with invalid input
print(foo('InvalidInput'))

```python
import ast

def foo(x):
    try:
        # Use ast.literal_eval() instead of eval()
        return ast.literal_eval(repr(x))
    except ValueError:
        raise ValueError("Invalid input: {}".format(x))

# Example usage for severity score
def severity_score(input_string):
    if not isinstance(input_string, str):
        raise TypeError("Input must be a string")

    try:
        x = foo(ast.literal_eval(input_string))
        # Determine the severity score based on the value of x
        if x == 0:
            return "Low"
        elif x < 5:
            return "Moderate"
        else:
            return "High"
    except ValueError as e:
        return str(e)

# Test cases
print(severity_score("42"))  # Output: Low
print(severity_score("'hello'"))  # Output: Moderate
print(severity_score("'malicious code'"))  # Output: High or an error message depending on the severity of the input
```

[16] Reflection Agent

```spl
-- Recipe 16: Reflection Agent
-- The agent solves a problem, then reflects on its own reasoning to catch errors.
-- If reflection finds issues, it corrects and re-reflects until confident.
-- Demonstrates meta-cognitive loops and self-assessment patterns.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 problem="What are the trade-offs of microservices vs monoliths?"
--   spl run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama problem="Design a URL shortener system"
--   spl run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama problem="Explain why bubble sort is O(n^2)"

WORKFLOW reflection_agent
    INPUT: @problem TEXT
    OUTPUT: @answer TEXT
DO
    @iteration := 0
    @max_reflections := 3
    @confidence := 0

    -- Initial attempt at solving the problem
    GENERATE solve(@problem) INTO @answer

    -- Reflection loop: assess and improve
    WHILE @iteration < @max_reflections DO
        -- Reflect: identify errors, gaps, or weak reasoning
        GENERATE reflect(@problem, @answer) INTO @reflection

        -- Score confidence in the answer
        GENERATE confidence_score(@answer, @reflection) INTO @confidence

        EVALUATE @confidence
            WHEN > 0.85 THEN
                COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'confident', confidence = @confidence, reflections = @iteration
            OTHERWISE
                -- Extract specific issues to fix
                GENERATE extract_issues(@reflection) INTO @issues

                -- Correct the answer based on identified issues
                GENERATE correct(@answer, @issues, @problem) INTO @answer

                @iteration := @iteration + 1
        END
    END

    -- If loop exhausted, commit best effort with final confidence
    COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'best_effort', confidence = @confidence, reflections = @iteration

EXCEPTION
    WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
        COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'max_reflections', confidence = @confidence
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        GENERATE solve(@problem) INTO @answer
        COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'restarted'
END
```

```output
**URL Shortener System Design**

The following is a design for a URL shortener system using Node.js and Express.js.

**System Overview:**

The URL shortener system allows users to input a long URL and generates a shortened version that can be used as an alternative. The system provides a simple interface for users to share the shortened URLs with others, making it easier for them to access the original content.

**Components:**

1.  **Frontend:**
    *   User Interface (UI): A web-based UI where users can input their long URL and submit it for shortening.
    *   API Endpoints:
        *   `POST /shorten`: Accepts a JSON body containing the long URL and returns the shortened URL as a response.
        *   `GET /clicks/{shortUrlCode}`: Returns the click count associated with the given shortened URL.

2.  **Backend:**
    *   Server-Side Logic:
        *   Handles incoming requests from the frontend (e.g., generating short URLs, retrieving click counts).
        *   Interacts with the database to store and retrieve data.
        *   Utilizes Redis for storing click counts, ensuring efficient updates and retrieval.

3.  **Database:**
    *   Stores information about shortened URLs, including their corresponding long URLs and click counts.
    *   Designed using a relational database management system (e.g., MySQL).

4.  **Redis:**
    *   Used to store click counts associated with shortened URLs, providing real-time updates on clicks.

**System Flow:**

1.  User Input:
    *   The user enters their long URL in the UI.
2.  Shortening Process:
    *   The frontend sends a POST request to `/shorten` endpoint with the long URL as JSON data.
    *   The backend verifies the input, generates a shortened URL using a secure hash function (e.g., SHA-256), and stores it in the database along with its corresponding long URL.
3.  Click Count Updates:
    *   When the user clicks on the shortened URL, their browser sends an HTTP request to `/clicks/{shortUrlCode}` endpoint.
4.  Click Count Retrieval and Update:
    *   The backend retrieves the click count associated with the given shortened URL from Redis.
    *   If there's no existing data in Redis for that shortened URL (i.e., it's a new click), the backend updates the click count in both Redis and the database.

**Security Considerations:**

1.  Input Validation:
    *   The system should validate incoming user input to prevent potential security vulnerabilities.
2.  Secure Hash Function:
    *   Use a secure hash function like SHA-256 when generating shortened URLs to ensure data integrity and uniqueness.
3.  Data Encryption (Optional):
    *   Consider implementing encryption mechanisms for sensitive data, such as the long URL or click counts.

**System Performance:**

1.  Connection Pooling:
    *   Implement connection pooling in the database to minimize resource usage and optimize performance.
2.  Click Count Updates Frequency:
    *   Balance the frequency of click count updates with system performance considerations (e.g., updating every few seconds).

By following this design, you can create a scalable and efficient URL shortener system that provides users with an easy-to-use interface for sharing shortened URLs while minimizing potential security vulnerabilities.

Example use cases:

*   A user wants to share the link `https://www.example.com/very-long-url` with their friends.
*   A developer uses the `/shorten` endpoint to generate a shortened URL (e.g., `http://localhost:3000/some-short-code`) for their own purposes.

This system can handle multiple users simultaneously, ensuring that each user's data is stored securely and efficiently.

     | ------------------------------------------------------------
     | Variables:
     |   @problem = Design a URL shortener system

```javascript
import express from 'express';
const app = express();
app.use(express.json());
require('dotenv').config();

// Database Connection Settings
const mysql = require('mysql2/promise');
const redis = require('redis');

const dbHost = process.env.DB_HOST || 'localhost';
const dbUser = process.env.DB_USER || 'your_username';
const dbPass = process.env.DB_PASS || 'your_password';

const shortCodeLength = 6;
const longUrlRegexp = /^https?:\/\/[^\/]+(\/.*)?$/;

// Initialize Database Connections
const redisClient = redis.createClient({
    host: process.env.REDIS_HOST,
    port: process.env.REDIS_PORT,
});

const db = mysql.createPool({
    host: dbHost,
    user: dbUser,
    password: dbPass,
    database: 'url_shortener',
});

// Secure Hash Function for Generating Short Codes
async function getShortCode(longUrl) {
    const crypto = require('crypto');
    return crypto.createHash('sha256')
        .update(longUrl)
        .digest('hex').substr(0, shortCodeLength);
}

app.post('/shorten', async (req, res) => {
    if (!req.body.longUrl || typeof req.body.longUrl !== 'string') {
        res.status(400).send('Invalid long URL');
        return;
    }

    const longUrl = req.body.longUrl;

    // Input Validation
    if (!longUrlRegexp.test(longUrl)) {
        res.status(400).send('Invalid long URL format');
        return;
    }

    try {
        const shortCode = await getShortCode(longUrl);
        const query = 'INSERT INTO short_urls SET ?';
        const values = { long_url: longUrl, short_code: shortCode };
        await db.execute(query, values);

        res.json({ shortUrl: `http://localhost:3000/${shortCode}` });
    } catch (error) {
        console.log('Error generating short URL:', error);
        res.status(500).send('Internal Server Error');
    }
});

app.get('/clicks/:shortUrlCode', async (req, res) => {
    const shortUrlCode = req.params.shortUrlCode;
    try {
        const query = 'SELECT * FROM short_urls WHERE short_code = ?';
        await db.execute(query, [shortUrlCode]);
        const result = await db.firstRow(query);
        if (!result) {
            throw new Error('Short URL not found');
        }

        // Update Click Count in Redis
        try {
            let clickCount = await redisClient.get(shortUrlCode);
            if (clickCount === null) {
                clickCount = 0;
            } else {
                clickCount = parseInt(clickCount);
            }
            clickCount++;
            await redisClient.set(shortUrlCode, clickCount.toString());
        } catch (error) {
            console.log('Error updating click count:', error);
        }

        // Update Click Count in MySQL Database
        const query = 'UPDATE short_urls SET clicks = ? WHERE short_code = ?';
        await db.execute(query, [clickCount + 1, shortUrlCode]);

        res.json({ clickCount: clickCount + 1 });
    } catch (error) {
        console.log('Error retrieving click count:', error);
        res.status(500).send('Internal Server Error');
    }
});

app.listen(3000, () => {
    console.log('Server listening on port 3000');
});
```
