  @issues = The code provided seems to be a basic implementation of a URL shortener system using Express.js and MySQL. However, there are several areas that can be improved for better security, performance, and scalability.

**Security:**

1.  **Input Validation:** The code validates the input URL using a regular expression pattern. While this is good practice, it's not sufficient to prevent all types of attacks. Consider using a more robust validation mechanism, such as using a library like `validity.js`.
2.  **Password Security:** The system uses environment variables for database connection settings, which is a good practice. However, consider using a secrets manager like AWS Secrets Manager or Hashicorp's Vault to store sensitive information securely.
3.  **Click Tracking:** The system stores click counts in both the MySQL database and Redis. While this provides redundancy, it can lead to inconsistencies if not handled properly. Consider using a more robust solution for storing and updating click counts.

**Performance and Scalability:**

1.  **Database Connection Pooling:** The code creates a new database connection pool instance each time the server starts. This can lead to performance issues and increased resource usage. Consider using connection pooling to improve performance.
2.  **Click Count Update Frequency:** The system updates click counts infrequently, which may not reflect real-time traffic. Consider implementing more frequent updates or using a more robust solution for storing click counts.

**Additional Improvements:**

1.  **Error Handling:** The code catches most errors and returns an internal server error response. Consider providing more informative error responses to help with debugging.
2.  **Logging:** The system does not log any information about the requests or database operations. Consider implementing logging mechanisms like Winston or Morgan to improve debuggability.
3.  **Input Sanitization:** The code does not sanitize user input, which can lead to security vulnerabilities. Consider using a library like `sanitize-html` to sanitize and escape HTML entities in user input.

Here's an updated version of the code with these improvements:

```spl
-- Recipe 17: Tree of Thought
-- Explores multiple reasoning paths in parallel, evaluates each, and selects the best.
-- Demonstrates branching exploration, comparative evaluation, and path scoring.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/17_tree_of_thought/tree_of_thought.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 problem="How should a startup prioritize between growth and profitability?"
--   spl run cookbook/17_tree_of_thought/tree_of_thought.spl --adapter ollama problem="Design a caching strategy for a high-traffic API"
--   spl run cookbook/17_tree_of_thought/tree_of_thought.spl --adapter ollama problem="Should we rewrite the legacy system or incrementally refactor?"

WORKFLOW tree_of_thought
    INPUT: @problem TEXT
    OUTPUT: @best_solution TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Generate multiple initial approaches
    GENERATE approach_1(@problem) INTO @path_a
    GENERATE approach_2(@problem) INTO @path_b
    GENERATE approach_3(@problem) INTO @path_c

    -- Step 2: Develop each approach one level deeper
    GENERATE develop(@path_a, @problem) INTO @path_a_developed
    GENERATE develop(@path_b, @problem) INTO @path_b_developed
    GENERATE develop(@path_c, @problem) INTO @path_c_developed

    -- Step 3: Score each path on multiple criteria
    GENERATE evaluate_path(@path_a_developed, @problem) INTO @score_a
    GENERATE evaluate_path(@path_b_developed, @problem) INTO @score_b
    GENERATE evaluate_path(@path_c_developed, @problem) INTO @score_c

    -- Step 4: Select the best path
    GENERATE select_best(
        @path_a_developed, @score_a,
        @path_b_developed, @score_b,
        @path_c_developed, @score_c
    ) INTO @best_path

    -- Step 5: Refine the winning path into a final solution
    GENERATE refine_solution(@best_path, @problem) INTO @best_solution

    -- Step 6: Verify the solution holds up
    GENERATE verify(@best_solution, @problem) INTO @verification

    EVALUATE @verification
        WHEN 'sound' THEN
            COMMIT @best_solution WITH status = 'complete', paths_explored = 3
        OTHERWISE
            -- If verification fails, combine insights from all paths
            GENERATE synthesize_all(
                @path_a_developed, @path_b_developed, @path_c_developed, @problem
            ) INTO @best_solution
            COMMIT @best_solution WITH status = 'synthesized', paths_explored = 3
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        -- Return whatever path was scored highest so far
        COMMIT @best_path WITH status = 'budget_limit'
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1
END

Based on the input provided, it seems that the decision to rewrite the legacy system or incrementally refactor depends on various factors such as the current state of the system, new business needs, and technological advancements. However, considering the pros and cons of each approach, incremental refactoring is suggested as a viable option.

Here are some key takeaways from the input:

1.  Incremental refactoring can provide reduced risk, improved maintainability, and flexibility compared to rewriting the entire system.
2.  Rewriting the legacy system can eliminate outdated or inefficient code, improve scalability, but comes with higher costs and increased complexity.
3.  A thorough analysis of the system's architecture is necessary to determine which parts require incremental refactoring and which might need a complete rewrite.

To make an informed decision, it would be helpful to assess the current state of the legacy system, evaluate the pros and cons based on specific circumstances, and consider conducting a comprehensive analysis of the system's architecture. This will enable you to strike a balance between rewriting and incremental refactoring, ensuring that your organization achieves its goals while minimizing disruption and optimizing resource allocation.

It is recommended to conduct further research and analysis to determine the best approach for your specific situation, taking into account factors such as:

1.  System complexity
2.  Current technology stack
3.  New business needs and requirements
4.  Available resources (time, budget, personnel)
5.  Potential risks and challenges

By considering these factors and conducting a thorough analysis, you can make an informed decision about whether to rewrite the legacy system or incrementally refactor, and choose the approach that best aligns with your organization's goals and objectives.

     | ------------------------------------------------------------
     | Variables:
     |   @problem = Should we rewrite the legacy system or incrementally refactor?
     |   @path_a = Approach 1: Rewrite vs Incremental Refactoring
     | 
     | When deciding whether to rewrite a legacy system or incrementally refactor it, consider the following factors:
     | 
     | **Rewrite (Big Bang Approach)**
     | 
     | Pros:
     | 
     | *   New architecture can provide better scalability and maintainability.
     | *   Can take advantage of modern technologies and design patterns.
     | 
     | Cons:
     | 
     | *   Involves significant upfront costs and resources.
     | *   May disrupt existing processes and workflows.
     | *   Can be challenging to predict the outcome and may lead to unforeseen problems.
     | 
     | **Incremental Refactoring (Phased Approach)**
     | 
     | Pros:
     | 
     | *   Allows for a gradual introduction of changes, reducing potential disruption to existing systems.
     | *   Enables testing and validation of individual components before moving on to the next phase.
     | *   Can help identify and address issues earlier in the process.
     | 
     | Cons:
     | 
     | *   May take longer to complete due to the phased approach.
     | *   Requires careful planning and management of multiple iterations.
     | *   Ensures that each component is thoroughly tested and validated before moving forward.
     | 
     | When deciding which approach to use, consider the following questions:
     | 
     | *   How critical is the legacy system to our business operations?
     | *   What are the current risks and pain points associated with the legacy system?
     | *   Are there any areas where modernization or replacement would significantly improve performance, scalability, or reliability?
     | *   Does the organization have the necessary resources (time, money, personnel) to invest in a rewrite, or can incremental refactoring be done incrementally?

     | Ultimately, the choice between rewriting and incrementally refactoring depends on your organization's specific circumstances, priorities, and capacity.

     | 
     | Considering the complexity of the legacy system and potential risks associated with rewriting it, I recommend an incremental refactoring approach. This method allows us to make targeted changes to the existing codebase while minimizing disruption to the overall system.
     | 
     | **Benefits:**
     | 
     | 1.  Reduced risk: By making incremental changes, we can test and validate each modification before introducing more significant changes.
     | 2.  Improved maintainability: Incremental refactoring enables us to focus on specific areas of the codebase at a time, reducing the complexity and scope of the project.
     | 3.  Flexibility: This approach allows us to adapt to changing requirements or new technologies without having to rewrite the entire system.
     | 
     | **Step-by-Step Plan:**
     | 
     | 1.  Identify critical components: Determine which parts of the legacy system are most critical to maintain and should be prioritized for refactoring first.
     | 2.  Break down complex tasks: Divide large, complex tasks into smaller, more manageable pieces, allowing us to focus on one aspect at a time.
     | 3.  Develop a phased plan: Create a step-by-step plan outlining the incremental changes to be made, along with timelines and resources required for each phase.
     | 4.  Perform unit testing and integration testing: Write comprehensive tests to ensure that each refactored component functions as expected before moving on to the next phase.
     | 5.  Collaborate and iterate: Work closely with stakeholders, developers, and other team members to gather feedback and refine the plan based on progress and results.
     |

     | 3.  Phase 3: Develop a phased plan (divide the task into smaller, more manageable pieces)

     | 
     | 1.  Business requirements: If new business needs require a complete overhaul of the system, rewriting may be justified.
     | 2.  Technological advancements: If newer technologies offer significant improvements over the existing system, rewriting might be advantageous.
     | 3.  Time and resource constraints: Consider whether sufficient time and resources are available to rewrite the entire system.

     | 
     | When deciding between rewriting a legacy system and incrementally refactoring it, there are several factors to consider.
     | 
     | **Rewrite the Legacy System:**
     | 
     | *   **Pros:** 
     |     *   A clean slate allows for a more comprehensive overhaul, potentially introducing new technologies or architectures that can improve performance, security, or scalability.
     |     *   Rewriting the system from scratch might be necessary if it's no longer maintainable with its current architecture.
     | *   **Cons:**
     |     *   This approach requires significant investment of time and resources.
     |     *   It may involve a steep learning curve for developers familiar with the existing codebase.
     | 
     | **Incremental Refactoring:**
     | 
     | *   **Pros:** 
     |     *   Incremental refactoring allows you to make targeted improvements, reducing the overall risk associated with rewriting the system.
     |     *   This approach enables developers to build upon their existing knowledge and expertise.
     | *   **Cons:**
     |     *   It may not address the root causes of the system's issues or limitations.
     |     *   The process can be time-consuming and labor-intensive.
     | 
     | **Recommendation**
     | 
     | Given the complexity of the legacy system, I recommend starting with an incremental refactoring approach. This will enable you to:
     | 
     | *   Identify and prioritize areas for improvement
     | *   Build momentum and confidence in the refactored codebase
     | *   Gather feedback from stakeholders and developers
     | 
     | Once you've made significant progress with incremental refactoring, reassess the system's overall architecture and consider rewriting it if necessary.
     | 
     | **Next Steps**
     | 
     | 1.  Conduct a thorough analysis of the legacy system, including its technical debt, performance issues, and scalability limitations.
     | 2.  Identify key areas for improvement and prioritize them based on business value and risk.
     | 3.  Develop a phased incremental refactoring plan, breaking down the work into manageable tasks and milestones.
     | 
     | By taking an incremental approach, you'll be able to make progress toward modernizing the legacy system while minimizing risks and maximizing the return on investment.
     | 
     | **Example Use Case**
     | 
     | Suppose we're working with a legacy system that's been in use for over a decade. During our analysis, we discover that the system is experiencing frequent crashes due to outdated dependencies.
     | 
     | To address this issue, we decide to start with an incremental refactoring approach. We begin by identifying and upgrading the outdated dependencies, making targeted improvements to stability and performance. As we make progress, we gather feedback from stakeholders and developers, refining our approach and prioritizing future work based on business needs.
     | 
     | Over time, we continue to refactor the system incrementally, addressing each of its limitations and improving its overall architecture. Eventually, we reassess the system's readiness for a complete rewrite, considering whether the benefits outweigh the costs.
     | 
     | **Code Example (Python)**
     | 
     | Here's an example of how you might implement incremental refactoring in Python:
```python
class LegacySystem:
    def __init__(self):
        self.dependencies = ["old_dependency1", "old_dependency2"]

    def process_data(self):
        # outdated logic
        pass

def refactor_legacy_system(legacy_system):
    # upgrade dependencies
    legacy_system.dependencies = ["new_dependency1", "new_dependency2"]
    
    # make targeted improvements to stability and performance
    return legacy_system

# create an instance of the LegacySystem
system = LegacySystem()

# start refactoring
refactored_system = refactor_legacy_system(system)

# continue refining the system incrementally
while True:
    # gather feedback from stakeholders and developers
    feedback = gather_feedback()
    
    # refine your approach based on business needs
    if feedback["impact"] > 0.5:
        break
    
    # prioritize future work based on business needs
    priority_work = get_priority_work()
    
    # continue refactoring
    system = refactor_legacy_system(system)
```

     | Before making a decision, consider the following factors:

     | 
     | **Evaluating the Options**
     | 
     | Weigh the pros and cons of each approach:
     | 
     | *   **Rewrite (Big Bang Approach)**:
     |     *   Pros: New architecture can provide better scalability and maintainability, take advantage of modern technologies and design patterns.
     |     *   Cons: Significant upfront costs and resources, potential disruption to existing processes and workflows, challenging to predict outcome.
     | *   **Incremental Refactoring (Phased Approach)**:
     |     *   Pros: Allows for gradual introduction of changes, reduces potential disruption to existing systems, enables testing and validation of individual components.
     |     *   Cons: May take longer to complete due to the phased approach, requires careful planning and management of multiple iterations.
     | 
     | **Choosing the Right Approach**
     | 
     | Based on your organization's specific circumstances, priorities, and capacity, decide whether:
     | 
     | 1.  **Rewriting is feasible**: If you have the necessary resources (time, money, personnel) and can afford the upfront costs, rewriting might be the better choice.
     | 2.  **Incremental refactoring is suitable**: If resources are limited or you want to maintain minimal disruption to existing systems, incremental refactoring could be a better option.
     | 
     | **Hybrid Approach**
     | 
     | Consider a hybrid approach that combines elements of both methods:
     | 
     | *   Start with incremental refactoring to address high-priority issues and test the waters.
     | *   Once incremental refactoring is complete, reassess the legacy system's condition and determine if rewriting would provide significant benefits.
     |

     | 3.  Flexibility: This approach allows you to adapt to changing requirements or new technologies without having to rewrite the entire system.
     | 
     | Cons:
     |

     | 
     | **Example Use Case**
     | 
     | Suppose we're working with a legacy system that's been in use for over a decade. During our analysis, we discover that the system is experiencing frequent crashes due to outdated dependencies.
     | 
     | To address this issue, we decide to start with an incremental refactoring approach. As we make progress, we gather feedback from stakeholders and developers, refining our approach and prioritizing future work based on business needs.
     | 
     | Over time, we continue to refactor the system incrementally, addressing each of its limitations and improving its overall architecture. Eventually, we reassess the system's readiness for a complete rewrite, considering whether the benefits outweigh the costs.
     | 
     | **Code Example (Python)**
     | 
     | Here's an updated example of how you might implement incremental refactoring in Python:
```python
class LegacySystem:
    def __init__(self):
        self.dependencies = ["old_dependency1", "old_dependency2"]

    def process_data(self):
        # outdated logic
        pass

def refactor_legacy_system(legacy_system):
    # upgrade dependencies
    legacy_system.dependencies = ["new_dependency1", "new_dependency2"]
    
    # make targeted improvements to stability and performance
    return legacy_system

# create an instance of the LegacySystem
system = LegacySystem()

# start refactoring
refactored_system = refactor_legacy_system(system)

# continue refining the system incrementally
while True:
    # gather feedback from stakeholders and developers
    feedback = gather_feedback()
    
    # refine your approach based on business needs
    if feedback["impact"] > 0.5:
        break
    
    # prioritize future work based on business needs
    priority_work = get_priority_work()
    
    # continue refactoring
    system = refactor_legacy_system(system)
```

     | Here's an updated example of how you might implement incremental refactoring in Python:
```python
class LegacySystem:
    def __init__(self):
        self.dependencies = ["old_dependency1", "old_dependency2"]

    def process_data(self):
        # outdated logic
        pass

def refactor_legacy_system(legacy_system):
    # upgrade dependencies
    legacy_system.dependencies = ["new_dependency1", "new_dependency2"]
    
    # make targeted improvements to stability and performance
    return legacy_system

# create an instance of the LegacySystem
system = LegacySystem()

# start refactoring
refactored_system = refactor_legacy_system(system)

# continue refining the system incrementally
while True:
    # gather feedback from stakeholders and developers
    feedback = gather_feedback()
    
    # refine your approach based on business needs
    if feedback["impact"] > 0.5:
        break
    
    # prioritize future work based on business needs
    priority_work = get_priority_work()
    
    # continue refactoring
    system = refactor_legacy_system(system)
```

```bash
spl run ./18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 user_input=Explain how encryption works
```
     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 18: Guardrails Pipeline
-- Input validation → safe generation → output validation → response.
-- Demonstrates safety-first patterns: content filtering, PII detection, and output sanitization.
-- Four gates before a response is committed.
--
-- Usage (from catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       input_id=safe_encryption
--
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       input_id=pii_ssn
--
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       input_id=harmful_malware
--
-- Usage (ad-hoc — no catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       user_input="My SSN is 123-45-6789, help me file taxes"
--
-- Discover available test inputs:
--   spl run ... input_id=list

WORKFLOW guardrails_pipeline
    INPUT:  @input_id   TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @user_input TEXT DEFAULT ''
    OUTPUT: @safe_response TEXT
    SECURITY: CLASSIFICATION: internal
DO
    -- Step 1: Load test input from catalog (deterministic — no LLM)
    -- If input_id is blank, @user_input is used directly in subsequent steps.
    CALL load_test_input(@input_id) INTO @test_context

    -- Gate 1a: Keyword pre-screen (deterministic — no LLM)
    -- Fast check for obvious harmful / off-topic patterns before spending tokens.
    CALL classify_input_keywords(@user_input) INTO @keyword_class

    EVALUATE @keyword_class
        WHEN STARTSWITH 'harmful' THEN
            COMMIT 'I cannot help with that request.' WITH
                status        = 'blocked_harmful',
                gate          = 'keyword_prescreen',
                keyword_match = @keyword_class
        WHEN STARTSWITH 'off_topic' THEN
            COMMIT 'That question is outside my scope. Please ask something relevant.' WITH
                status        = 'blocked_off_topic',
                gate          = 'keyword_prescreen',
                keyword_match = @keyword_class
        OTHERWISE
            -- Continue to LLM classification
    END

    -- Gate 1b: LLM input content classification (nuanced cases keywords miss)
    GENERATE classify_input(@user_input, @test_context) INTO @input_class

    EVALUATE @input_class
        WHEN 'harmful' THEN
            COMMIT 'I cannot help with that request.' WITH
                status = 'blocked_harmful',
                gate   = 'llm_classifier'
        WHEN 'off_topic' THEN
            COMMIT 'That question is outside my scope. Please ask something relevant.' WITH
                status = 'blocked_off_topic',
                gate   = 'llm_classifier'
        OTHERWISE
            -- Input is acceptable, continue
    END

    -- Gate 2: PII detection (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL detect_pii(@user_input) INTO @pii_report

    EVALUATE @pii_report
        WHEN STARTSWITH 'pii_found' THEN
            -- Gate 2b: Redact PII (deterministic — no LLM)
            CALL redact_pii(@user_input) INTO @clean_input
        OTHERWISE
            SET @clean_input = @user_input
    END

    -- Gate 3: Generate response with safety-tuned prompt
    GENERATE safe_response(@clean_input) INTO @raw_response

    -- Gate 4: Output validation — check for PII leakage or harmful content
    GENERATE validate_output(@raw_response, @clean_input) INTO @output_check

    EVALUATE @output_check
        WHEN 'safe' THEN
            SET @safe_response = @raw_response
        WHEN 'contains_pii' THEN
            -- Deterministic PII redaction on the output too
            CALL redact_pii(@raw_response) INTO @safe_response
        WHEN 'hallucination' THEN
            GENERATE safe_response(@clean_input) INTO @safe_response
            RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1
        OTHERWISE
            SET @safe_response = 'I was unable to generate a safe response. Please rephrase your question.'
    END

    COMMIT @safe_response WITH
        status       = 'complete',
        input_class  = @input_class,
        pii_detected = @pii_report

EXCEPTION
    WHEN RefusalToAnswer THEN
        COMMIT 'This request cannot be processed.' WITH status = 'refused'
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        COMMIT 'Unable to provide a verified response.' WITH status = 'hallucination_blocked'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT @safe_response WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

     result: FAILED  (0.1s)

[19] Memory Conversation

```
     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 19: Memory-Augmented Conversation
-- A conversational agent that remembers facts from prior turns using persistent memory.
-- Demonstrates memory.get/memory.set, context accumulation, and stateful interactions.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama user_input="My name is Alice and I'm a data scientist"
--   spl run cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama user_input="What's my name?"
--   spl run cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama user_input="I prefer Python over R"
--   spl run cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama user_input="What do you know about me?"
--
-- Memory persists across runs via `spl memory` store.
-- Reset with: spl memory delete chat_user_profile

WORKFLOW memory_conversation
    INPUT: @user_input TEXT
    OUTPUT: @response TEXT
DO
    -- Load existing user profile from memory
    SELECT memory.get('chat_user_profile') AS profile INTO @profile
    SELECT memory.get('chat_history') AS chat_history INTO @chat_history

    -- Extract any new facts the user shared about themselves
    GENERATE extract_facts(@user_input) INTO @new_facts

    -- Update profile with new facts (if any)
    EVALUATE @new_facts
        WHEN 'no_new_facts' THEN
            -- Nothing to update
        OTHERWISE
            GENERATE merge_profile(@profile, @new_facts) INTO @profile
            -- STORE @profile IN memory.chat_user_profile  -- workflow-level memory write not yet supported
    END

    -- Generate response with full context: profile + history + current input
    GENERATE contextual_reply(@user_input, @profile, @chat_history) INTO @response

    -- Update chat history (keep last 10 turns)
    @chat_history := @chat_history + '\nUser: ' + @user_input + '\nAssistant: ' + @response
    GENERATE trim_history(@chat_history, 10) INTO @chat_history
    -- STORE @chat_history IN memory.chat_history  -- workflow-level memory write not yet supported

    COMMIT @response WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT 'I remember you! But I ran out of budget for this response.' WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

```spl
-- Recipe 20: Ensemble Voting
-- Generates multiple independent answers, then uses majority voting to pick the best.
-- Demonstrates ensemble methods, comparative scoring, and consensus-driven output.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 question="What causes inflation?"
--   spl run cookbook/20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama question="Is Rust faster than C++?"
--   spl run cookbook/20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama question="What is the best database for time-series data?"

WORKFLOW ensemble_voting
    INPUT: @question TEXT
    OUTPUT: @final_answer TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Generate 5 independent candidate answers with varying temperatures
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_1
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_2
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_3
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_4
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_5

    -- Step 2: Score each candidate on accuracy, completeness, and clarity
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_1, @question) INTO @score_1
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_2, @question) INTO @score_2
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_3, @question) INTO @score_3
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_4, @question) INTO @score_4
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_5, @question) INTO @score_5

    -- Step 3: Find consensus — identify common themes across candidates
    GENERATE find_consensus(
        @candidate_1, @candidate_2, @candidate_3,
        @candidate_4, @candidate_5
    ) INTO @consensus

    -- Step 4: Select the best candidate based on scores and consensus alignment
    GENERATE select_winner(
        @candidate_1, @score_1,
        @candidate_2, @score_2,
        @candidate_3, @score_3,
        @candidate_4, @score_4,
        @candidate_5, @score_5,
        @consensus
    ) INTO @best_candidate

    -- Step 5: Polish the winning answer, incorporating consensus insights
    GENERATE polish(@best_candidate, @consensus, @question) INTO @final_answer

    COMMIT @final_answer WITH status = 'complete', candidates = 5

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        -- If budget runs out, use whatever candidates we have
        GENERATE select_winner(
            @candidate_1, @score_1,
            @candidate_2, @score_2,
            @candidate_3, @score_3
        ) INTO @final_answer
        COMMIT @final_answer WITH status = 'partial', candidates = 3
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1
END
```

```output
import re
from collections import defaultdict

class ScoreCandidate:
    def __init__(self):
        self.inflation_causes = [
            "Demand and supply imbalance",
            "Monetary policy",
            "Economic growth",
            "Commodity price shocks",
            "Supply chain disruptions"
        ]

    def score_candidate(self, answer):
        correct_score = 0
        for cause in self.inflation_causes:
            if re.search(cause.lower(), answer.lower(), re.IGNORECASE):
                correct_score += 1

        return correct_score

    def find_common_themes(self, inputs):
        common_themes = set()
        themes = ["monetary policy", "demand and supply imbalance", "economic growth", "commodity price shocks"]

        for input in inputs:
            theme_matches = [i for i, t in enumerate(themes) if re.search(t.lower(), input['answer'].lower())]
            
            if len(theme_matches) > 0:
                common_themes.update([themes[i] for i in theme_matches])
        
        return list(common_themes)

    def analyze_inputs(self, inputs):
        scores = {}
        commonthemes = self.find_common_themes(inputs)
        
        for input in inputs:
            score = self.score_candidate(input['answer'])
            scores[input['name']] = {'score': score}
            
            if 'commonThemes' in input:
                input['commonThemes'] = list(set(commonthemes) & set(input['commonThemes']))
        
        return scores

# Example usage:

inputs = [
    {"name": "Candidate 1", "answer": "Monetary policy and demand and supply imbalance are two main causes of inflation"},
    {"name": "Candidate 2", "answer": "Economic growth is a cause of inflation, as well as commodity price shocks"},
    {"name": "Candidate 3", "answer": "Demand-pull inflation and cost-push inflation can both contribute to inflation"},
]

score_candidate = ScoreCandidate()
scores = score_candidate.analyze_inputs(inputs)

for name, result in scores.items():
    print(f"Name: {name}, Score: {result['score']}")
    if 'commonThemes' in result:
        print("Common Themes:", result['commonThemes'])
```

```python
class ScoreCandidate:
    def __init__(self):
        self.inflation_causes = [
            "Demand and supply imbalance",
            "Monetary policy",
            "Economic growth",
            "Commodity price shocks",
            "Supply chain disruptions"
        ]

    def score_candidate(self, answer):
        correct_score = 0
        for cause in self.inflation_causes:
            if cause.lower() in answer.lower():
                correct_score += 1

        return correct_score

    def find_commonthemes(self, inputs):
        common_themes = []
        themes = ["monetary policy", "demand and supply imbalance", "economic growth", "commodity price shocks"]
        
        for input in inputs:
            theme_matches = [i for i, t in enumerate(themes) if t.lower() in input.lower()]
            
            if len(theme_matches) > 0:
                common_themes.append([t for i, t in enumerate(themes) if i in theme_matches])
        
        return common_themes

    def analyze_inputs(self, inputs):
        scores = {}
        commonthemes = self.find_commonthemes(inputs)
        
        for input in inputs:
            score = self.score_candidate(input['answer'])
            scores[input['name']] = {'score': score}
            
            if 'commonThemes' in input:
                input['commonThemes'] = commonthemes
        
        return scores

# Example usage:

inputs = [
    {"name": "Candidate 1", "answer": "Monetary policy and demand and supply imbalance are two main causes of inflation"},
    {"name": "Candidate 2", "answer": "Economic growth is a cause of inflation, as well as commodity price shocks"},
    {"name": "Candidate 3", "answer": "Demand-pull inflation and cost-push inflation can both contribute to inflation"},
]

score_candidate = ScoreCandidate()
scores = score_candidate.analyze_inputs(inputs)

for name, result in scores.items():
    print(f"Name: {name}, Score: {result['score']}")
    if 'commonThemes' in result:
        print("Common Themes:", result['commonThemes'])
```
     | 
     | This code defines a class `ScoreCandidate` that encapsulates the logic for scoring candidates and identifying common themes among their answers. The `analyze_inputs` method scores each candidate based on their answer, finds common themes among their responses, and returns the results as a dictionary.
     | 
     | The example usage demonstrates how to create an instance of the `ScoreCandidate` class, provide inputs with answers, and analyze these inputs to determine scores and identify common themes.

```spl
-- Recipe 21: Multi-Model Pipeline
-- Showcases GENERATE ... USING MODEL per-step model selection
-- and semantic WHILE loop with variable context.
--
-- Each step can target a different model — the right model for each task.
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/21_multi_model_pipeline/multi_model.spl --adapter ollama topic="climate change"

CREATE FUNCTION research(topic TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a research specialist.
Provide key facts, statistics, and recent developments about: {topic}
Focus on factual accuracy. Be thorough but concise.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION analyze(facts TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a data analyst.
Analyze the following research and identify the 3 most significant insights:

{facts}

For each insight, explain why it matters and rate its significance (1-10).
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION write_summary(analysis TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a professional writer.
Write a clear, engaging 2-paragraph summary based on this analysis:

{analysis}

The first paragraph should present the key findings.
The second paragraph should discuss implications and outlook.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION quality_check(text TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a quality reviewer.
Rate the following text on a scale of 0.0 to 1.0 for:
- Clarity
- Accuracy
- Completeness

Return ONLY a single number (the average score).

Text: {text}
$$;

WORKFLOW multi_model_pipeline
  INPUT: @topic TEXT
  OUTPUT: @final TEXT
DO
  -- Step 1: Research with a fast model (good for factual retrieval)
  GENERATE research(@topic) USING MODEL 'gemma3' INTO @facts;

  -- Step 2: Analysis with a reasoning model
  GENERATE analyze(@facts) USING MODEL 'gemma3' INTO @analysis;

  -- Step 3: Writing with a creative model
  GENERATE write_summary(@analysis) USING MODEL 'gemma3' INTO @draft;

  -- Step 4: Quality loop — refine until score > 0.7 (max 3 iterations)
  @iteration := 0;
  @quality := 0;

  WHILE @iteration < 3 DO
    GENERATE quality_check(@draft) INTO @quality;

    EVALUATE @quality
      WHEN > 0.7 THEN
        COMMIT @draft WITH status = 'high_quality', score = @quality
      OTHERWISE
        GENERATE write_summary(@analysis) INTO @draft
        @iteration := @iteration + 1
    END
  END

  -- Fallback: commit whatever we have
  COMMIT @draft WITH status = 'max_iterations', score = @quality

EXCEPTION
  WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
    COMMIT @draft WITH status = 'partial'
  WHEN ModelOverloaded THEN
    COMMIT @draft WITH status = 'model_overloaded'
END;
```

```output
Here’s a two-paragraph summary based on the provided climate change analysis:

The latest scientific evidence overwhelmingly confirms that human activity is the primary driver of climate change, establishing an “unequivocal” influence on the global climate system. Supported by dramatic increases in greenhouse gas concentrations – particularly carbon dioxide – alongside observed global temperature rises and a clear correlation between the two, this insight fundamentally shifts the conversation away from debate and towards actionable solutions. Furthermore, the analysis reveals a concerning acceleration in global warming, with the last decade being the warmest on record and a direct link established between rising ocean temperatures and increasingly intense extreme weather events like heatwaves, droughts, and wildfires. Finally, the research underscores a critical, shrinking carbon budget, indicating that we’ve already consumed a significant portion of our allowable emissions, demanding immediate and drastic decarbonization across all sectors.

These findings paint a stark picture with significant implications for the future. The accelerating rate of warming and the escalating frequency of extreme weather events necessitate immediate and substantial investment in adaptation strategies and disaster preparedness. Simultaneously, the exhaustion of the carbon budget emphasizes the urgency of transitioning to a low-carbon economy. Successfully navigating this challenge requires rapid advancements in renewable energy technologies, widespread adoption of sustainable practices, and ambitious policy decisions designed to drastically reduce emissions within the very near term—a critical window of opportunity that, if missed, will dramatically worsen the consequences of climate change for generations to come.
```
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
     | Variables:
     |   @topic = climate change
     |   @facts = Okay, here’s a thorough but concise overview of climate change, incorporating key facts, statistics, and recent developments, focusing on factual accuracy as a research specialist would present it:
     | 
     | **I. The Science – What’s Happening?**
     | 
     | * **The Greenhouse Effect:** The Earth’s atmosphere naturally traps some of the sun’s energy, warming the planet. This is essential for life. However, human activities have significantly amplified this effect.
     | * **Greenhouse Gas Increases:**  Concentrations of key greenhouse gases – carbon dioxide (CO2), methane (CH4), nitrous oxide (N2O), and fluorinated gases – have risen dramatically since the Industrial Revolution. 
     |     * **CO2:**  Currently at 421 parts per million (ppm) in 2023, significantly higher than pre-industrial levels of around 280 ppm.  This is primarily due to the burning of fossil fuels (coal, oil, and natural gas) and deforestation.
     |     * **Methane:**  A more potent short-term greenhouse gas. Levels have increased by roughly 90% since 1970.  Sources include agriculture (livestock), natural gas and petroleum systems, and decaying organic matter.
     |     * **Nitrous Oxide:** Primarily from agricultural practices (fertilizer use) and industrial processes. 
     | * **Observed Changes:** The Earth is unequivocally warming.
     |     * **Global Average Temperature Rise:** Since 1880, the Earth has warmed by approximately 1.1°C (2.0°F). The last decade (2014-2023) was the warmest on record.
     |     * **Sea Level Rise:**  Rising temperatures cause thermal expansion of water and melting of glaciers and ice sheets, contributing to a global average sea level rise of approximately 21-26 cm (8-10 inches) since 1880. The rate of rise is accelerating.
     |     * **Extreme Weather Events:** Increased frequency and intensity of extreme weather events, including:
     |         * **Heatwaves:** Becoming more frequent and hotter.
     |         * **Heavy Rainfall & Flooding:**  Increased intensity of rainfall events.
     |         * **Droughts:**  Longer and more severe droughts in many regions.
     |         * **Wildfires:**  Increased frequency and intensity due to hotter, drier conditions.
     |         * **Hurricanes & Tropical Cyclones:**  Some research suggests a link between warmer ocean temperatures and increased intensity.
     | 
     | 
     | **II. Statistics & Key Indicators (as of late 2023/early 2024)**
     | 
     | * **IPCC Reports:** The Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC) – the leading international body for assessing climate change – regularly publishes assessment reports. The Sixth Assessment Report (AR6) concluded with “unequivocal” evidence of human influence on the climate system.
     | * **Global Temperature Increase:**  The World Meteorological Organization (WMO) reported that 2023 was the warmest year on record by a significant margin.
     | * **Carbon Budget:** We’ve used roughly 60% of our remaining carbon budget (the amount of CO2 we can emit while still having a 50% chance of limiting warming to 1.5°C) according to Climate Action Tracker.
     | * **Renewable Energy Growth:** Renewable energy sources (solar, wind, hydro) accounted for approximately 30% of global electricity generation in 2023, a significant increase, but still insufficient to meet global needs.
     | 
     | 
     | **III. Recent Developments & Trends**
     | 
     | * **Ocean Warming:** Oceans have absorbed over 90% of the excess heat trapped by greenhouse gases. Ocean temperatures are rising rapidly, impacting marine ecosystems.
     | * **Arctic Amplification:** The Arctic is warming at roughly twice the global average rate, leading to rapid sea ice melt and thawing of permafrost. Permafrost thaw releases significant amounts of methane and CO2.
     | * **Climate Litigation:**  Increasing legal challenges against governments and corporations for their contributions to climate change.
     | * **Carbon Capture & Storage (CCS):**  Growing interest and development of technologies to capture CO2 emissions from industrial sources and store them underground. However, scalability and cost remain significant challenges.
     | * **Net-Zero Commitments:** Many countries and companies have pledged to achieve “net-zero” emissions by mid-century. This requires not only reducing emissions but also removing CO2 from the atmosphere.
     | 
     | 
     | **IV. Sources for Further Information (Reliable & Regularly Updated)**
     | 
     | * **IPCC:** [https://www.ipcc.ch/](https://www.ipcc.ch/)
     | * **NASA Climate Change:** [https://climate.nasa.gov/](https://climate.nasa.gov/)
     | *
     |   @analysis = Okay, here’s an analysis of the provided climate change research, identifying the 3 most significant insights, along with explanations and significance ratings:
     |

```spl
-- Recipe 23: Structured Output
-- Extract typed, structured data from free-form text using CREATE FUNCTION with JSON schema.
-- Demonstrates schema-constrained generation — the LLM must return valid JSON matching the spec.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/23_structured_output/structured_output.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       text="John Smith, 42, joined Acme Corp in March 2021 earning $95,000/year"
--
--   spl run cookbook/23_structured_output/structured_output.spl --adapter ollama \
--       text="Invoice #INV-2045 from TechSupplies Ltd dated 15 Jan 2024 for $3,420.50 due in 30 days"

-- Define the extraction schema
CREATE FUNCTION extract_entity_schema()
RETURNS JSON AS $$
{
  "type": "object",
  "properties": {
    "people": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "name":       { "type": "string" },
          "age":        { "type": "integer" },
          "role":       { "type": "string" },
          "salary":     { "type": "number" },
          "start_date": { "type": "string", "format": "date" }
        },
        "required": ["name"]
      }
    },
    "organizations": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "name":     { "type": "string" },
          "type":     { "type": "string" },
          "industry": { "type": "string" }
        },
        "required": ["name"]
      }
    },
    "amounts": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "value":    { "type": "number" },
          "currency": { "type": "string", "default": "USD" },
          "label":    { "type": "string" }
        },
        "required": ["value"]
      }
    },
    "dates": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "value": { "type": "string" },
          "label": { "type": "string" }
        },
        "required": ["value"]
      }
    }
  }
}
$$;

PROMPT extract_entities
SELECT
    system_role('You are a precise data extraction engine. Extract all entities from the text and return valid JSON matching the provided schema. Return only the JSON object, no explanation.'),
    context.text AS text,
    extract_entity_schema() AS schema
GENERATE structured_extraction(text, schema)
```

     | Model: gemma3
     | Tokens: 96 in / 74 out
     | Latency: 1209ms
     | Cost: $0.000000
     | ------------------------------------------------------------

{
  "name": "John Smith",
  "age": 42,
  "company": "Acme Corp",
  "joining_date": "March 2021",
  "salary": 95000,
  "salary_frequency": "year"
}

```spl
-- Recipe 24: Few-Shot Prompting
-- Embed gold-standard examples directly in the SELECT context to guide output format and style.
-- Demonstrates in-context learning — no fine-tuning required.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/24_few_shot/few_shot.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       text="The quarterly results exceeded all analyst forecasts by a significant margin"
--
--   spl run cookbook/24_few_shot/few_shot.spl --adapter ollama \
--       text="System outage detected in EU-WEST-2 affecting 15% of users" \
--       domain="ops"

-- Gold-standard examples for tone/format calibration
CREATE FUNCTION few_shot_examples(domain TEXT DEFAULT 'general')
RETURNS TEXT AS $$
SELECT CASE domain
  WHEN 'finance' THEN
    '### Examples

Input: "Revenue grew 12% YoY despite headwinds in APAC."
Output: {"sentiment": "positive", "magnitude": 0.7, "topics": ["revenue", "growth", "APAC"], "summary": "Revenue up 12% YoY."}

Input: "Write-downs of $2.3B drove a net loss for Q3."
Output: {"sentiment": "negative", "magnitude": 0.9, "topics": ["write-downs", "net loss", "Q3"], "summary": "Q3 net loss from $2.3B write-downs."}'

  WHEN 'ops' THEN
    '### Examples

Input: "Deployment succeeded. All health checks passing. P99 latency 45ms."
Output: {"status": "healthy", "severity": "none", "action_required": false, "summary": "Deploy OK, latency nominal."}

Input: "Pod crash-loop in prod-east. OOMKilled. Restarted 7 times in 10 min."
Output: {"status": "incident", "severity": "high", "action_required": true, "summary": "OOM crash-loop in prod-east, immediate attention needed."}'

  ELSE
    '### Examples

Input: "The new feature launched today and users are loving it!"
Output: {"sentiment": "positive", "magnitude": 0.8, "summary": "Successful feature launch with positive user reception."}

Input: "We encountered several critical bugs that delayed the release by two weeks."
Output: {"sentiment": "negative", "magnitude": 0.7, "summary": "Critical bugs caused two-week release delay."}'
END
$$;

PROMPT few_shot_classifier
SELECT
    system_role('You are a precise text classifier. Follow the examples exactly — same JSON structure, same field names. Return only the JSON object.'),
    few_shot_examples(context.domain) AS examples,
    context.text AS text,
    context.domain AS domain
GENERATE classify(text, examples)
```
     | 
     | ============================================================
     | Model: gemma3
     | Tokens: 77 in / 35 out
     | Latency: 610ms
     | Cost: $0.000000
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
```output

{
  "text": "The quarterly results exceeded all analyst forecasts by a significant margin",
  "domain": "finance"
}
```

     | Log: /home/gongai/.spl/logs/few_shot-ollama-20260322-233952.log
     result: SUCCESS  (0.8s)

spl run ./25_nested_procs/nested_procs.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic=quantum computing audience=high school students
```
     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 25: Nested Procedures
-- Demonstrates PROCEDURE calling PROCEDURE — deep composability without external orchestration.
-- Pattern: a high-level workflow delegates subtasks to reusable PROCEDURE units.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/25_nested_procs/nested_procs.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       topic="quantum computing" audience="high school students"
--
--   spl run cookbook/25_nested_procs/nested_procs.spl --adapter ollama \
--       topic="CRISPR gene editing" audience="policy makers" depth="deep"

-- Inner PROCEDURE: generate a single explanation layer
PROCEDURE explain_layer(content TEXT, audience TEXT, style TEXT DEFAULT 'clear and engaging')
RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE explain(content, audience, style) INTO @explanation
    COMMIT @explanation
END

-- Inner PROCEDURE: generate a concrete example for a concept
PROCEDURE make_example(concept TEXT, context TEXT, audience TEXT)
RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE concrete_example(concept, context, audience) INTO @example
    COMMIT @example
END

-- Inner PROCEDURE: check comprehension level and simplify if needed
PROCEDURE calibrate_complexity(text TEXT, audience TEXT, target_grade INT DEFAULT 8)
RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE assess_reading_level(text) INTO @reading_level
    EVALUATE @reading_level
        WHEN > target_grade THEN
            GENERATE simplify(text, audience) INTO @calibrated
            COMMIT @calibrated
        OTHERWISE
            COMMIT text
    END
END

-- Outer WORKFLOW: orchestrates the inner procedures
WORKFLOW layered_explainer
    INPUT: @topic TEXT, @audience TEXT, @depth TEXT DEFAULT 'standard'
    OUTPUT: @article TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Research the topic
    GENERATE research_overview(@topic) INTO @overview

    -- Step 2: Delegate explanation to inner procedure
    CALL explain_layer(
        content  = @overview,
        audience = @audience,
        style    = 'clear, engaging, avoid jargon'
    ) INTO @base_explanation

    -- Step 3: Generate a concrete example via another inner procedure
    CALL make_example(
        concept  = @topic,
        context  = @base_explanation,
        audience = @audience
    ) INTO @example

    -- Step 4: Calibrate complexity (nested call inside a conditional)
    CALL calibrate_complexity(
        text         = @base_explanation,
        audience     = @audience,
        target_grade = 8
    ) INTO @calibrated_explanation

    -- Step 5: Assemble the final article
    GENERATE assemble_article(
        @topic,
        @calibrated_explanation,
        @example,
        @depth
    ) INTO @article

    COMMIT @article WITH status = 'complete', audience = @audience
END
```
     | 
     | ============================================================
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 5
     | Tokens: 2565 in / 2765 out
     | Latency: 37364ms
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
```output
Here's an assembled article based on your inputs:

**What is Quantum Computing?**

Imagine you have a super powerful calculator that can solve problems way faster and more accurately than any regular computer. That's basically what quantum computing is - a new way of doing calculations using the weird and wonderful rules of the tiny world of atoms.

**How Does it Work?**

Classical computers use "bits" to store and process information, which can only be either 0 or 1 at a time. But quantum computers use special "qubits" that can be both 0 AND 1 at the same time! This means they can try many possibilities all at once, making them super fast and powerful.

**What Can We Do with Quantum Computing?**

Quantum computing has the potential to revolutionize many areas of our lives. Here are a few examples:

* **Cryptography**: Quantum computers can break some kinds of encryption, but they can also be used to create new, unbreakable codes.
* **Optimization problems**: Quantum computers can help us solve complex problems in fields like machine learning and logistics, which could lead to breakthroughs in areas like image recognition and natural language processing.
* **Simulation**: Quantum computers can simulate complex systems, which could lead to major discoveries in chemistry and materials science.

**What's Holding Us Back?**

There are still some big challenges to overcome before we can use quantum computing for all its potential. These include:

* **Noise and error correction**: Quantum computers are prone to errors due to the fragile nature of qubits.
* **Scalability**: Most current quantum computers are tiny, so we need to figure out how to build larger systems.

**What's Next?**

Researchers are working on developing new technologies that can help overcome these challenges. Some areas of focus include:

* **Quantum-classical hybrids**: Combining the strengths of both classical and quantum computing.
* **Topological quantum computing**: Exploring new materials and concepts for more robust quantum computing architectures.

By understanding how quantum computing works and what its potential is, we can start to explore ways to harness its power for the benefit of humanity.

**Quantum Computing for High School Students**

Imagine you have a super powerful calculator that can solve problems way faster and more accurately than any regular computer. That's basically what quantum computing is - a new way of doing calculations using the weird and wonderful rules of the tiny world of atoms.

**How Can Quantum Computing Help Us?**

Quantum computing has the potential to revolutionize many areas of our lives. Here are a few examples:

* **Code Breaking**: Quantum computers can help us break some kinds of encryption, but they can also be used to create new, unbreakable codes.
* **Optimization Problems**: Quantum computers can help us solve complex problems in fields like machine learning and logistics, which could lead to breakthroughs in areas like image recognition and natural language processing.
* **Simulation**: Quantum computers can simulate complex systems, which could lead to major discoveries in chemistry and materials science.

**What Can You Do with Quantum Computing?**

As a high school student, you might be wondering how you can get involved with quantum computing. Here are a few ways:

* **Learn more about coding**: Understanding programming languages like Python or Java will help you understand how quantum computers work.
* **Participate in hackathons or competitions**: Join online communities or participate in local events to learn from other students and professionals in the field.
* **Research and explore**: Read articles, watch videos, and talk to experts to learn more about the latest developments in quantum computing.

**What's the Future of Quantum Computing?**

Researchers are working on developing new technologies that can help overcome the challenges of quantum computing. Some areas of focus include:

* **Quantum-classical hybrids**: Combining the strengths of both classical and quantum computing.
* **Topological quantum computing**: Exploring new materials and concepts for more robust quantum computing architectures.

By understanding how quantum computing works and what its potential is, you can start to explore ways to harness its power for the benefit of humanity. Who knows? Maybe one day you'll be part of a team that helps create a new technology that changes the world!

     |

     | 2. **Superposition**: Qubits can exist in a superposition of states, allowing them to process multiple possibilities simultaneously.
     | 3. **Entanglement**: Qubits can become entangled, enabling the creation of complex quantum states and facilitating communication between particles.
     | 4. **Quantum gates**: Quantum computing uses quantum gates, which are the quantum equivalent of logic gates in classical computing.

     |

     | * **Optimization problems**: Quantum computers can help us solve complex problems in fields like machine learning and logistics, which could lead to breakthroughs in areas like image recognition and natural language processing.
     | * **Simulation**: Quantum computers can simulate complex systems, which could lead to major discoveries in chemistry and materials science.
     | 
     | **What's Holding Us Back?**
     | 
     | There are still some big challenges to overcome before we can use quantum computing for all its potential. These include:
     | 
     | * **Noise and error correction**: Quantum computers are prone to errors due to the fragile nature of qubits.
     | * **Scalability**: Most current quantum computers are tiny, so we need to figure out how to build larger systems.
     | 
     | **What's Next?**
     | 
     | Researchers are working on developing new technologies that can help overcome these challenges. Some areas of focus include:
     | 
     | * **Quantum-classical hybrids**: Combining the strengths of both classical and quantum computing.
     | * **Topological quantum computing**: Exploring new materials and concepts for more robust quantum computing architectures.
     | 
     | By understanding how quantum computing works and what its potential is, we can start to explore ways to harness its power for the benefit of humanity.

```spl
-- Recipe 26: Prompt A/B Test
-- Compare two prompt variants using EVALUATE scoring. Pick the winner automatically.
-- Demonstrates data-driven prompt engineering built into the workflow itself.
-- Supports pre-built experiments from experiments.json or ad-hoc task + prompt_a + prompt_b.
--
-- Usage (from catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/26_ab_test/ab_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/26_ab_test/tools.py \
--       experiment_id=neural_networks
--
--   spl run cookbook/26_ab_test/ab_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/26_ab_test/tools.py \
--       experiment_id=standing_desk winner_threshold=2.0
--
-- Usage (ad-hoc — no catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/26_ab_test/ab_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/26_ab_test/tools.py \
--       task="Explain neural networks" \
--       prompt_a="Explain like I'm 5" \
--       prompt_b="Give a technical explanation with analogies"
--
-- Discover available experiments:
--   spl run ... experiment_id=list

-- Scoring rubric as a reusable function
CREATE FUNCTION scoring_rubric()
RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Rate the response on a scale of 0–10 for each criterion:
1. Clarity (0-10): Is it easy to understand?
2. Completeness (0-10): Does it fully address the task?
3. Relevance (0-10): Is every sentence on-topic?
4. Engagement (0-10): Is it interesting to read?

Return valid JSON only — no prose outside the JSON:
{"clarity": N, "completeness": N, "relevance": N, "engagement": N, "total": N, "rationale": "one concise sentence"}
$$;

WORKFLOW ab_test
    INPUT:  @experiment_id    TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @task             TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @prompt_a         TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @prompt_b         TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @winner_threshold FLOAT DEFAULT 1.5
    OUTPUT: @winner_output TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Load experiment from catalog (deterministic — no LLM)
    -- Sets @experiment_context with task, prompt_a, prompt_b, and notes.
    -- If experiment_id is blank, the context will be empty and @task/@prompt_a/@prompt_b are used directly.
    CALL load_experiment(@experiment_id) INTO @experiment_context

    -- Step 2: Generate Variant A response
    GENERATE run_variant_a(
        @task,
        @prompt_a,
        @experiment_context
    ) INTO @response_a

    -- Step 3: Generate Variant B response
    GENERATE run_variant_b(
        @task,
        @prompt_b,
        @experiment_context
    ) INTO @response_b

    -- Step 4: Score both variants against the rubric (LLM)
    GENERATE evaluate_response(@response_a, @task, scoring_rubric()) INTO @score_a_json
    GENERATE evaluate_response(@response_b, @task, scoring_rubric()) INTO @score_b_json

    -- Step 5: Extract numeric totals (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL extract_score_total(@score_a_json) INTO @score_a
    CALL extract_score_total(@score_b_json) INTO @score_b

    -- Step 6: Pick winner or declare tie
    EVALUATE (@score_a - @score_b)
        WHEN > @winner_threshold THEN
            SET @winner_output = @response_a
            COMMIT @winner_output WITH
                winner    = 'A',
                score_a   = @score_a,
                score_b   = @score_b,
                margin    = (@score_a - @score_b)
        WHEN < (0 - @winner_threshold) THEN
            SET @winner_output = @response_b
            COMMIT @winner_output WITH
                winner    = 'B',
                score_a   = @score_a,
                score_b   = @score_b,
                margin    = (@score_b - @score_a)
        OTHERWISE
            -- Scores too close — format both with explanation (deterministic — no LLM)
            CALL format_tie_result(@response_a, @response_b, @score_a_json, @score_b_json) INTO @winner_output
            COMMIT @winner_output WITH
                winner  = 'tie',
                score_a = @score_a,
                score_b = @score_b
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        COMMIT 'A/B test failed during generation.' WITH status = 'error'
END
```

     | LLM Calls: 8

```output
def format_tie_result():
    # Initialize variables
    tie_score = ""
    tie_message = ""

    # Check if there is a tie
    if score1 > score2 and score2 > score3:
        tie_score = "Tie"
        tie_message = "The scores are tied between Input 1, Input 2, and Input 3."
    elif score1 > score2 and score2 > score3:
        tie_score = "Input 1 wins"
        tie_message = f"Input 1 has a higher score than both Input 2 and Input 3."
    elif score2 > score1 and score1 > score3:
        tie_score = "Input 2 wins"
        tie_message = f"Input 2 has a higher score than both Input 1 and Input 3."
    else:
        tie_score = "Input 3 wins"
        tie_message = f"Input 3 has a higher score than both Input 1 and Input 2."

    return tie_score, tie_message

# Example usage
score1, score2, score3 = 80, 90, 70
tie_score, tie_message = format_tie_result()
print(f"Tie Score: {tie_score}")
print(f"Tie Message: {tie_message}")

     |   @response_a = Here's an explanation of neural networks that explains like you're 5:
     | 
     | Imagine you have a toy box full of blocks in different shapes and sizes. You want to teach a robot how to sort these blocks into different boxes based on their shape and size.
     | 
     | A neural network is like a special kind of computer brain that can learn from examples, just like the robot would. It's made up of many layers of interconnected nodes (or "neurons") that process information in a specific way.
     | 
     | Here's how it works:
     | 
     | 1. You show the robot a block and say, "This one goes in the red box!"
     | 2. The robot looks at the block and tries to figure out what makes it special.
     | 3. It uses its brain (the neural network) to compare the block to all the other blocks it's seen before.
     | 4. If it finds a match, like another block that's also red, it says, "Ah-ha! This one goes in the red box!"
     | 5. The robot keeps doing this with each new block it sees, and its brain gets better and better at sorting them into boxes.
     | 
     | The magic happens when you show the robot many blocks and tell it what goes where. Its brain starts to learn patterns and rules that help it make predictions about which block belongs in a particular box.
     | 
     | In the end, if you teach the robot enough blocks, its brain will be so smart that it can sort them all by itself!
     | 
     | Now, let's talk about how neural networks are used for real-world applications like computer vision and machine learning.
     |   @response_b = I'd be happy to explain neural networks from both an intuitive and technical perspective!
     | 
     | **Neural Networks: An Intuitive Explanation**
     | 
     | Imagine you're trying to recognize cats in pictures. You show the picture of a cat to many people, and ask them if it's a cat or not. Most of them will say yes. Now, imagine that instead of showing the pictures yourself, you have a special machine that can look at the pictures on its own.
     | 
     | This machine is like a super-smart version of you, and it uses something called "connections" to figure out what's in the picture. These connections are like little roads that help the machine understand the picture better.
     | 
     | The machine has three layers of these connections:
     | 
     | 1. **Input Layer**: This layer looks at all the information in the picture, like edges, shapes, and colors.
     | 2. **Hidden Layers**: These layers use the input layer's information to make decisions about what's in the picture. It's like a game of "connect-the-dots" where each connection represents a decision.
     | 3. **Output Layer**: This final layer says "yes, this is a cat!" or "no, it's not a cat." Based on all the connections made by the hidden layers.
     | 
     | When you train this machine, you show it many pictures and tell it which ones are cats. The machine learns to make decisions based on these examples, and its connections get stronger and more accurate over time.
     | 
     | **Neural Networks: A Technical Explanation**
     | 
     | A neural network is a type of machine learning algorithm that's inspired by the structure and function of the human brain. It consists of layers of interconnected nodes or "neurons" that process information.
     | 
     | Here's a technical overview:
     | 
     | 1. **Artificial Neurons (Node)**: Each node receives one or more inputs, performs a computation on those inputs, and then sends the output to other nodes.
     | 2. **Connections (Edges)**: Nodes are connected to each other through edges, which represent the strength of the connection between them.
     | 3. **Activation Functions**: When a node receives an input, it applies an activation function to determine whether to keep or discard that input.
     | 
     | The key components of a neural network include:
     | 
     | * **Convolutional Layers**: These layers use convolutional filters to extract features from images or other data.
     | * **Recurrent Layers**: These layers use feedback connections to allow the model to capture temporal relationships in data, such as time series data.
     | * **Fully Connected Layers**: These layers are used for classification and regression tasks.
     | 
     | When you train a neural network, you:
     | 
     | 1. **Feedforward Pass**: The input is passed through each layer, with weights and biases applied at each step.
     | 2. **Backpropagation**: The error between the predicted output and actual output is calculated, and then propagated backwards to adjust the weights and biases of each layer.
     | 
     | This process continues until the network converges, meaning that its performance on a validation set stops improving.
     | 
     | In practice, neural networks are used for a wide range of tasks, including:
     | 
     | * Image classification
     | * Natural language processing (NLP)
     | * Time series forecasting
     | * Regression
     | 
     | Neural networks have many advantages, such as:
     | 
     | * **Flexibility**: Neural networks can be applied to a wide range of tasks and domains.
     | * **Scalability**: Neural networks can handle large amounts of data and complex computations.
     | 
     | However, neural networks also have some limitations, such as:
     | 
     | * **Computational Cost**: Training deep neural networks requires significant computational resources.
     | * **Overfitting**: Neural networks can overfit the training data if not regularized properly.

```python
def extract_score_total(scoring_rubric):
    """
    Evaluates two input responses and returns their total score.
    
    Args:
        scoring_rubric: An object containing methods for evaluating explanations.
        
    Returns:
        The total score of both inputs.
    """

    # Initialize the total score to 0
    total_score = 0

    # Evaluate Input 1: Explain neural networks in simple terms
    input1_explanation_score = scoring_rubric.evaluate_explanation("explain_neural_networks", "Input 1 explanation")
    
    # Add the score to the total score
    total_score += input1_explanation_score
    
    # Check if the response matches the expected explanation
    if "blocks" in input1_explanation_score and "neural network" in input1_explanation_score:
        print("Input 1 has all required keywords")
    else:
        print("Input 1 is missing one or more required keywords")

    # Evaluate Input 2: Explain neural networks without using simple terms
    input2_explanation_score = scoring_rubric.evaluate_explanation("explain_neural_networks", "Input 2 explanation")
    
    # Add the score to the total score
    total_score += input2_explanation_score
    
    # Check if the response matches the expected explanation
    if "layers of interconnected nodes" in input2_explanation_score and "compare blocks" in input2_explanation_score:
        print("Input 2 has all required keywords")
    else:
        print("Input 2 is missing one or more required keywords")

    return total_score

# Example usage
class ScoringRubric:
    def evaluate_explanation(self, topic, response):
        # Replace with actual scoring logic
        if topic == "explain_neural_networks" and len(response.split()) > 10:
            return 1
        else:
            return 0

scoring_rubric = ScoringRubric()
total_score = extract_score_total(scoring_rubric)
print("Total Score:", total_score)
```

```spl
-- Recipe 27: Data Extraction
-- Pull structured fields from messy text: names, dates, amounts, references.
-- Demonstrates schema-constrained extraction on real-world noisy input.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/27_data_extraction/data_extraction.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       text="Please process payment of USD 4,250.00 to Riverside Consulting (ref: PO-8821) by end of March."
--
--   spl run cookbook/27_data_extraction/data_extraction.spl --adapter ollama \
--       text="$(cat invoice.txt)" format="invoice"

CREATE FUNCTION extraction_schema(format TEXT DEFAULT 'general')
RETURNS JSON AS $$
SELECT CASE format
  WHEN 'invoice' THEN '{
    "type": "object",
    "properties": {
      "invoice_number": {"type": "string"},
      "vendor":         {"type": "string"},
      "amount":         {"type": "number"},
      "currency":       {"type": "string", "default": "USD"},
      "issue_date":     {"type": "string", "format": "date"},
      "due_date":       {"type": "string", "format": "date"},
      "line_items":     {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "object", "properties": {"description": {"type": "string"}, "amount": {"type": "number"}}}},
      "payment_terms":  {"type": "string"},
      "po_reference":   {"type": "string"}
    }
  }'
  WHEN 'contract' THEN '{
    "type": "object",
    "properties": {
      "parties":        {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
      "effective_date": {"type": "string", "format": "date"},
      "expiry_date":    {"type": "string", "format": "date"},
      "value":          {"type": "number"},
      "currency":       {"type": "string"},
      "jurisdiction":   {"type": "string"},
      "key_obligations":{"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}}
    }
  }'
  ELSE '{
    "type": "object",
    "properties": {
      "names":        {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
      "organizations":{"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
      "dates":        {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "object", "properties": {"value": {"type": "string"}, "label": {"type": "string"}}}},
      "amounts":      {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "object", "properties": {"value": {"type": "number"}, "currency": {"type": "string"}, "label": {"type": "string"}}}},
      "references":   {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
      "locations":    {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}}
    }
  }'
END
$$;

PROMPT extract_fields
SELECT
    system_role('You are a data extraction specialist. Extract all relevant fields from the text and return valid JSON matching the schema exactly. If a field is not present in the text, omit it. Return only the JSON object.'),
    context.text AS text,
    extraction_schema(context.format) AS schema,
    context.format AS format
GENERATE extract(text, schema)
```

     | Model: gemma3
     | Tokens: 114 in / 64 out
     | Latency: 1056ms

}
